▸ 9.1 Pain Physiology Assessment 8
▸ 9.1.01 Neuropathic Pain Overview And Assessment Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Pain Physiology & Assessment

1. 1. Definition

Neuropathic pain is pain caused by a lesion or disease of the somatosensory system. Features: burning, electric, shooting, pins-and-needles, numbness, allodynia (pain from non-painful stimulus), hyperalgesia (exaggerated pain). Causes: diabetic neuropathy, post-herpetic neuralgia, trigeminal neuralgia, central post-stroke, cancer infiltration, nerve injury. Screening tools: LANSS, DN4, painDETECT. Treatment: TCAs (amitriptyline), SNRIs (duloxetine), gabapentinoids (gabapentin, pregabalin), tramadol, topical capsaicin/lidocaine, weak then strong opioids.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

See Section 1 entry; this is the dedicated section 4 file with focus on clinical assessment and management of neuropathic pain conditions.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

LANSS, DN4, painDETECT, QST, MRI neuroaxis, NCS/EMG, bloods (B12, folate, glucose, autoimmune, paraneoplastic).

2. Management

First-line: gabapentin 300-3600mg/day, pregabalin 150-600mg/day, duloxetine 60-120mg/day, amitriptyline 25-150mg. Second-line: combination, tramadol, topical lidocaine 5%, capsaicin 8%. Interventional: SCS, PNS, nerve blocks.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
DefinitionPain from lesion/disease of somatosensory system
FeaturesBurning, electric, shooting, allodynia, hyperalgesia
CausesDiabetic, PHN, trigeminal, post-stroke, cancer
Prevalence7-10% general population
ScreeningLANSS, DN4, painDETECT
First-lineTCAs, SNRIs, gabapentinoids (NICE, 2013)
TopicalCapsaicin (8% patch for PHN), lidocaine 5%
OpioidsTramadol third-line; strong opioids if severe (last-line)
NICETCAs OR duloxetine OR pregabalin OR gabapentin

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
NociceptiveTissue damage; not in nerve distribution

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: LANSS score? A: ≥12 = neuropathic pain.
  1. 2. Q: DN4 score? A: ≥4 = neuropathic pain.
  1. 3. Q: First-line treatments? A: Gabapentin, pregabalin, duloxetine, amitriptyline.
  1. 4. Q: Spinal cord stimulation indication? A: Failed back surgery syndrome, refractory CRPS, neuropathic pain.
  1. 5. Q: Capsaicin 8% patch? A: Single 60min application, effective for PHN and diabetic neuropathy.

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.1.02 Nociceptive Pain Somatic And Visceral Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Pain Physiology & Assessment

1. 1. Definition

Nociceptive pain is pain from actual or threatened tissue damage activating nociceptors. Subtypes: somatic (sharp, well-localised, Aδ fibres — skin, bone, muscle, joint) and visceral (dull, deep, poorly localised, referred, C fibres — organs, hollow viscera). Common causes: trauma, surgery, inflammation, cancer infiltration, obstruction. Treatment: paracetamol, NSAIDs, opioids, treatment of underlying cause.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Nociceptive pain arises from actual or threatened damage to non-neural tissue with intact somatosensory system. Somatic pain: well-localised, sharp, aching, throbbing; worsens with movement; examples: bone metastases, post-operative, fracture, osteoarthritis. Visceral pain: poorly localised, deep, cramping, squeezing, pressure; often referred to dermatomes (e.g., diaphragmatic irritation → shoulder tip via phrenic C3, C4, C5; cardiac → left arm T1-T2; gallbladder → right shoulder/scapula); associated autonomic features (sweating, nausea, vomiting, pallor); examples: bowel obstruction, pancreatic cancer, liver capsule distension, ureteric colic. Clinical features include guarding, rigidity, tenderness, and signs of underlying pathology. Visceral pain often has 'silent' areas with hyperalgesia in the referred zone (Head's zones).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

History (SOCRATES — Site, Onset, Character, Radiation, Associations, Timing, Exacerbating/relieving, Severity), examination (inspection, palpation, percussion, auscultation), vital signs. Disease-specific: full blood count (infection, inflammation), ESR/CRP, U&E (renal colic, metabolic), LFTs, amylase (pancreatitis), troponin (cardiac), imaging (X-ray, CT, ultrasound, MRI), endoscopy (OGD, colonoscopy), laparoscopy if indicated. Pain assessment tools: NRS, VAS, McGill, BPI.

2. Management

Treat underlying cause (surgery, chemotherapy, radiotherapy for cancer; antibiotics for infection). WHO analgesic ladder: paracetamol ± NSAID for mild; weak opioid (codeine, tramadol) for moderate; strong opioid (morphine, oxycodone, fentanyl) for severe. Adjuvants: corticosteroids for liver capsule pain, bone pain, raised ICP; antispasmodics (hyoscine, mebeverine) for smooth muscle colic; laxatives for opioid-induced constipation; antiemetics. Non-pharmacological: TENS, heat, massage, positioning. Interventional: coeliac plexus block (pancreatic), nerve blocks, epidural.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
TypeTissue damage activating nociceptors
SomaticSharp, well-localised, Aδ
VisceralDull, deep, referred, C
ExamplesSomatic: fracture, RA, OA, post-op; visceral: MI, appendicitis, renal colic
TreatmentParacetamol, NSAIDs, opioids, treat cause

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Somatic painWell-localised, sharp
Visceral painPoorly localised, cramping, referred

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: What is the difference between somatic and visceral pain? A: Somatic = well-localised, sharp, from skin/muscle/bone; visceral = poorly localised, deep, cramping, often referred, from organs.
  1. 2. Q: What is referred pain and examples? A: Visceral pain perceived in somatic dermatome; e.g., diaphragmatic irritation → shoulder (C3, C4, C5); cardiac ischaemia → left arm T1-T2; gallbladder → right scapula.
  1. 3. Q: First-line for visceral pain? A: Treat cause + WHO ladder + adjuvant (corticosteroid for organ capsule, antispasmodic for colic, opioid for severe).
  1. 4. Q: Coeliac plexus block indication? A: Pancreatic cancer pain; often >70% pain relief.
  1. 5. Q: What is Head's zone? A: Dermatome of cutaneous hyperalgesia corresponding to a visceral organ's nerve supply.

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.1.03 Pain Assessment History And Examination Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Pain Physiology & Assessment

1. 1. Definition

Pain assessment in palliative care requires comprehensive history (SOCRATES + oncology-specific: site, severity, character, timing, aggravating/relieving, previous treatment, impact on function/mood/sleep) and examination. Multidimensional tools: Brief Pain Inventory (BPI), McGill Pain Questionnaire. Pain in non-communicating patients: PAINAD, Doloplus-2, FLACC (for children). Assessment must consider total pain (physical, emotional, social, spiritual).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Pain history uses SOCRATES (Site, Onset, Character, Radiation, Associations, Timing, Exacerbating/relieving, Severity) plus cancer-specific (incident, breakthrough, end-of-dose failure). Functional impact: activities of daily living, work, sleep, mood, relationships. Psychosocial: beliefs, fears, cultural, spiritual. Examination: observation (posture, facial expression, gait, skin changes), palpation (tenderness, trigger points, allodynia), percussion, range of motion, neurological (sensation, motor, reflexes), musculoskeletal, abdominal. Severity by patient report on 0-10 scale. Impact: BPI, QoL.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Pain assessment tools: NRS, VAS, VRS, McGill, BPI, PAIN-AD (dementia), FLACC (non-verbal children), PPP (paediatric). Disease-specific: imaging, bloods, neurophysiology. Functional: Barthel Index, Karnofsky Performance Status, ECOG.

2. Management

Comprehensive assessment is the foundation of effective pain management: takes time, builds trust, identifies cause. Use validated tools. Address psychosocial and spiritual (total pain). Reassess regularly and document.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
HistorySOCRATES + cancer-specific: site, severity, character
ToolsBPI, McGill Pain Questionnaire
Non-communicatingPAINAD, Doloplus-2, FLACC
MultidimensionalPhysical, emotional, social, spiritual
FunctionImpact on ADL, sleep, mood, work
ExaminationSite, radiation, neuro exam, trigger points
Re-assessmentAfter every intervention; document response

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
N/AAssessment framework

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: What is SOCRATES for pain history? A: Site, Onset, Character, Radiation, Associations, Timing, Exacerbating/relieving, Severity.
  1. 2. Q: Best tool for non-communicating patient? A: PAIN-AD (Pain in Advanced Dementia) or FLACC (children).
  1. 3. Q: Components of total pain? A: Physical + psychological + social + spiritual.
  1. 4. Q: Why use validated pain tools? A: Standardised, comparable, sensitive to change, audit.
  1. 5. Q: Cancer pain sub-types? A: Background, breakthrough, incident, end-of-dose failure.

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  1. SOCRATES = Site, Onset, Character, Radiation, Associations, Time course, Exacerbating/relieving, Severity

▸ 9.1.04 Pain Assessment Tools And Scales Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Pain Physiology & Assessment

1. 1. Definition

Pain assessment tools include unidimensional (severity only) and multidimensional scales. Unidimensional: Visual Analogue Scale (VAS, 0-100mm), Numerical Rating Scale (NRS, 0-10), Verbal Rating Scale (VRS, none/mild/moderate/severe), Faces Pain Scale (children, Wong-Baker). Multidimensional: Brief Pain Inventory (BPI), McGill Pain Questionnaire. Special populations: PAINAD (dementia), FLACC (children), Abbey Pain Scale (non-verbal adults). Severity categories: mild 1-3, moderate 4-6, severe 7-10.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Validated pain assessment tools: NRS (0-10, most common in adults), VAS (0-100mm, sensitive), VRS (none/mild/moderate/severe), McGill Pain Questionnaire (sensory, affective, evaluative), BPI (severity and interference with function, 0-10), LANSS and DN4 (screening for neuropathic), PAIN-AD (5 items: breathing, vocalisation, facial expression, body language, consolability, 0-10), FLACC (Face, Legs, Activity, Cry, Consolability, 0-10, paediatric), PPP (paediatric). Functional impact: Barthel Index, ADLs. QoL: EORTC QLQ-C30, FACT-G, EQ-5D. Cancer-specific: Edmonton Symptom Assessment Scale (ESAS), Karnofsky, ECOG.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Document with each clinical review. For non-communicating patients, use observational tools. For cognitively impaired, use PAIN-AD. For dementia, Abbey Pain Scale. For children, use age-appropriate (FLACC, Wong-Baker faces).

2. Management

Use consistent tool. Reassess regularly. Combine self-report (gold standard) with behavioural/physiological measures when needed. Document intervention-response. Total pain: physical + psychological + social + spiritual.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Best tool for adult? A: NRS (0-10) — simple, validated, sensitive.
  1. 2. Q: Best tool for dementia? A: PAIN-AD.
  1. 3. Q: Best tool for non-communicating ICU? A: Behavioural Pain Scale (BPS) or Critical-Care Pain Observation Tool (CPOT).
  1. 4. Q: Tool for breakthrough pain? A: Brief Pain Inventory (BPI) assesses both background and breakthrough.
  1. 5. Q: What is VAS? A: Visual Analogue Scale 0-100mm horizontal line, patient marks pain intensity.

▸ 9.1.05 Pain In Non Communicating And Dementia Patients Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Pain Physiology & Assessment

1. 1. Definition

Pain assessment in non-communicating patients (dementia, stroke, learning disability, intubated, end of life) is challenging as self-report is not possible. Use observational tools: PAINAD (Pain Assessment IN Advanced Dementia), Abbey Pain Scale, Doloplus-2 (elderly), FLACC (children), BPS (Behavioural Pain Scale, ICU). Behavioural indicators: facial expression (grimacing), vocalisation (moaning, calling out), body movement (guarding, rigidity), behaviour (agitation, aggression), physiological (tachycardia, hypertension).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Pain in non-communicating and dementia patients is under-recognised and under-treated. Signs: facial expression (grimacing, frowning), vocalisation (groaning, moaning, calling out, asking for help), body language (guarding, rubbing, restlessness, rigidity), behavioural changes (aggression, agitation, withdrawal, sleep disturbance, refusal of care, decreased appetite), physiological (tachycardia, hypertension, sweating). Tools: PAIN-AD (Pain in Advanced Dementia, 5 items 0-10 each), Abbey Pain Scale (6 items), Doloplus-2, BPS (Behavioural Pain Scale for ICU), CPOT (Critical-Care Pain Observation Tool).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Behavioural and physiological observation; proxy report from carers/family; consider common pain triggers (positioning, hygiene, procedures, continence care). Disease-specific: full blood count, ESR, CRP, imaging for occult fracture, urinary retention (bladder scan), faecal impaction, oral thrush, pressure ulcers.

2. Management

Identify cause (thorough assessment using tools), treat cause, regular paracetamol (low threshold), regular opioid (if pain suspected, trial with careful titration and monitoring), topical (lignocaine patch, capsaicin for localised). Non-pharmacological: positioning, comfortable environment, music, touch, massage. Treat delirium, anxiety, depression. Family involvement, communication. Trial of analgesia with monitoring (N-of-1 trial).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
PAINADPain Assessment IN Advanced Dementia; 5 items 0-2
Abbey6 items; 0-3 each
Doloplus-2Elderly; 10 items
FLACCChildren 2 months-7y; 5 items
BPSICU; 3 items
BehaviouralFacial, vocal, body movement
PhysiologicalHR, BP, RR
TrialTrial of analgesia if in doubt

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: How to assess pain in advanced dementia? A: PAIN-AD scale; behavioural observation; family/carer report.
  1. 2. Q: Why is pain under-treated in dementia? A: Communication difficulties, atypical presentation, attribution to disease/behavioural issues, lack of tools.
  1. 3. Q: Trial of analgesia principle? A: If pain suspected, treat and monitor; if no improvement, consider non-pain cause.
  1. 4. Q: Common under-recognised pain in dementia? A: Musculoskeletal, dental, constipation, urinary retention, pressure ulcers, fracture (especially post-fall).
  1. 5. Q: PAIN-AD components? A: Breathing, vocalisation, facial expression, body language, consolability.

▸ 9.1.06 Pain Physiology And Pathways Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Pain Physiology & Assessment

1. 1. Definition

Pain is defined by the IASP as 'an unpleasant sensory and emotional experience associated with, or resembling that associated with, actual or potential tissue damage'. Pain pathways involve nociception (transduction), transmission, modulation, and perception. Nociceptors (Aδ and C fibres) detect noxious stimuli; transmission via dorsal horn → spinothalamic tract → thalamus → somatosensory cortex (sensory), limbic system (emotional). Modulation by descending pathways (periaqueductal grey → rostroventral medulla → dorsal horn), endogenous opioids, serotonin, noradrenaline. Central sensitisation (wind-up) amplifies pain in chronic states.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Pain is a complex sensory and emotional experience with both nociceptive and neuropathic components. Nociceptors (free nerve endings) are activated by mechanical, thermal, and chemical stimuli, with Aδ fibres mediating fast, sharp, well-localised pain, and C fibres mediating slow, dull, burning, poorly-localised pain. Clinical features of acute pain: sympathetic activation (tachycardia, hypertension, sweating, dilated pupils), protective behaviour, muscle spasm, anxiety. Chronic pain (>3 months): maladaptation, depression, sleep disturbance, functional impairment, autonomic changes, often no sympathetic activation. Pain types: nociceptive (somatic — well-localised, sharp; visceral — diffuse, cramping, referred), neuropathic (shooting, burning, electric, with allodynia, hyperalgesia, dysaesthesia), mixed. Central sensitisation explains the wind-up phenomenon in chronic pain (NMDA-mediated, hyperexcitable dorsal horn neurons).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Clinical assessment with validated tools: NRS (numerical 0-10), VAS (visual analogue 0-100mm), VRS (verbal rating, none/mild/moderate/severe), McGill Pain Questionnaire, BPI (Brief Pain Inventory), LANSS (Leeds Assessment of Neuropathic Symptoms and Signs), DN4 (Douleur Neuropathique 4), PAIN-AD (Pain in Advanced Dementia). Disease-specific: imaging (X-ray, CT, MRI for bone/visceral/neuropathic), nerve conduction studies, blood tests (ESR, CRP, autoimmune, infection, metabolic), quantitative sensory testing (QST).

2. Management

Multimodal approach: treat underlying cause; total pain concept (physical + psychological + social + spiritual); WHO analgesic ladder with adjuvants for neuropathic; non-pharmacological (TENS, CBT, physiotherapy, acupuncture, exercise, mindfulness); interventional (nerve blocks, spinal cord stimulation, intrathecal pump); psychological support; address sleep, depression, anxiety; family/social support.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
IASPUnpleasant sensory and emotional experience
NociceptorsAδ (fast, sharp) and C (slow, dull) fibres
TransductionStimulus → electrical signal via ion channels (TRPV1, Nav1.7)
TransmissionDorsal horn → spinothalamic → thalamus → cortex
ModulationDescending: PAG, RVM, dorsal horn; endorphins, serotonin, NA
PerceptionSensory cortex (somatic), limbic (emotional), frontal (cognitive)
Central sensitisationWind-up; NMDA-mediated; chronic pain
Gate controlMelzack & Wall, 1965; large fibre activation inhibits small fibre
Endogenous opioidsEnkephalins, endorphins, dynorphins; μ, δ, κ receptors
NeuropathicLesion/disease of somatosensory system
NociceptiveActual or threatened tissue damage

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Nociceptive painSomatic/visceral; tissue damage; opioid-responsive
Neuropathic painNerve damage; burning/shooting; LANSS/DN4+
Nociplastic painNo tissue/nerve damage (e.g., fibromyalgia); central sensitisation
Psychogenic painPsychiatric; somatoform; no organic basis

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: What is the IASP definition of pain? A: An unpleasant sensory and emotional experience associated with, or resembling that associated with, actual or potential tissue damage.
  1. 2. Q: Which fibres mediate fast and slow pain? A: Aδ fibres = fast, sharp, well-localised; C fibres = slow, dull, burning.
  1. 3. Q: What is central sensitisation? A: NMDA-mediated hyperexcitability of dorsal horn neurons in chronic pain, leading to wind-up and allodynia.
  1. 4. Q: Name 4 ascending/descending pain pathways. A: Ascending: spinothalamic (lateral — pain/temp; medial — crude touch); spinoreticular; trigeminothalamic. Descending: PAG → rostroventral medulla → dorsal horn (serotonin, NA, endogenous opioids).
  1. 5. Q: What is the difference between nociceptive and neuropathic pain? A: Nociceptive = tissue damage, well-localised, opioid-responsive; neuropathic = nerve damage, burning/shooting, opioid-poorly-responsive, requires adjuvants (gabapentin, TCA, SNRI).

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  1. T-T-M-P = Transduction, Transmission, Modulation, Perception

▸ 9.1.08 Total Pain Concept Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Pain Physiology & Assessment

1. 1. Definition

Total pain (Cicely Saunders) is the recognition that pain in advanced disease is not just physical but encompasses emotional, social, spiritual, and existential suffering. Components: physical (disease, treatment), emotional (depression, anxiety, fear), social (family, isolation, finances), spiritual (meaning, faith, hope), existential (loss, death). Assessment requires a multidisciplinary team. Treatment requires addressing all dimensions — pharmacological (analgesics) AND non-pharmacological (counselling, spiritual care, family support).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Total pain concept (Cicely Saunders, founder of modern palliative care) recognises that pain has multiple components: physical (tissue damage, nerve, organ), psychological (anxiety, depression, fear, anger), social (loss of role, financial, isolation, family conflict), spiritual (existential distress, loss of meaning, fear of death). All components interact. Clinical features: complex pain, refractory to opioids alone, requires multimodal team, family involvement, addressing non-physical components is essential.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Holistic assessment: physical, psychological, social, spiritual. Use total pain framework. Tools: HADS, ESAS, FICA, HOPE, PHQ-9, GAD-7. Multidisciplinary team assessment.

2. Management

Multimodal, multidisciplinary: physical (analgesia, adjuvants, interventional); psychological (CBT, counselling, relaxation, mindfulness); social (social work, family meetings, financial support, carer support); spiritual (chaplain, faith community, existential exploration). Family involvement. Address fears and concerns. Listen and validate.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ConceptCicely Saunders; multidimensional pain
PhysicalDisease, treatment, immobility
EmotionalDepression, anxiety, fear, loss
SocialFamily burden, isolation, finances
SpiritualMeaning, faith, hope, despair
ExistentialLoss, death, identity, dignity
AssessmentMDT: medical, nursing, psychology, social work, chaplaincy
TreatmentPharmacological + non-pharmacological

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: What is the total pain concept? A: Pain with physical, psychological, social, and spiritual components, all interacting (Cicely Saunders).
  1. 2. Q: Why address all components? A: Pain in one component amplifies others; multimodal approach is more effective than opioids alone.
  1. 3. Q: Who is Cicely Saunders? A: Founder of modern palliative care, St Christopher's Hospice (1967), developed total pain concept.
  1. 4. Q: Example of social pain? A: Loss of role, financial worries, isolation, family conflict.
  1. 5. Q: Example of spiritual pain? A: Loss of meaning, fear of death, existential distress, loss of faith.

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.2 NonOpioid Analgesics 5
▸ 9.2.01 Cox 2 Inhibitors Coxibs Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Non-Opioid Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

COX-2 selective inhibitors (coxibs) selectively inhibit COX-2, reducing GI side effects (ulcer, bleed) vs traditional NSAIDs. Examples: celecoxib, etoricoxib, parecoxib (IV). However, INCREASED cardiovascular risk (MI, stroke, hypertension) led to rofecoxib (Vioxx) withdrawal in 2004. Use: patients with high GI risk, but caution in CV risk. NICE: coxibs with PPI cover for high GI risk patients. Avoid in established CV disease.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

COX-2 selective inhibitors (celecoxib, etoricoxib) preferentially inhibit COX-2 (inflammatory), sparing COX-1 (gastric protection, platelet function). Less GI ulceration/bleeding, but increased CV events (myocardial infarction, stroke, hypertension, heart failure) — rofecoxib (Vioxx) withdrawn 2004. Use in patients with high GI risk and lower CV risk. Indication: osteoarthritis, RA, dysmenorrhoea, gout (etoricoxib), acute pain. Not for CV prevention.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

CV risk assessment before use (avoid if recent MI, unstable angina, recent CABG, stroke, severe heart failure), renal function (caution eGFR<30, contraindicated <15), BP monitoring, GI risk. Sulphonamide allergy (celecoxib contraindicated).

2. Management

Celecoxib 100-200mg OD-BD; etoricoxib 30-120mg OD. Lowest effective dose, shortest time. Reassess CV risk. Add PPI if high GI risk. Avoid in established CV disease. Monitor BP, renal function.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ExamplesCelecoxib, etoricoxib, parecoxib (IV)
MechanismSelective COX-2
GILess GI bleed vs traditional NSAID
CVINCREASED MI, stroke, hypertension
WithdrawnRofecoxib (Vioxx) 2004
CurrentCelecoxib, etoricoxib; caution in CV risk
NICEWith PPI for high GI risk

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Traditional NSAIDMore GI risk, less CV risk
ParacetamolNo anti-inflammatory

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Why was Vioxx withdrawn? A: Increased CV events (myocardial infarction, stroke) with long-term use.
  1. 2. Q: Difference vs traditional NSAID? A: Less GI ulceration/bleeding (COX-1 sparing), but similar or higher CV risk.
  1. 3. Q: When to use coxib? A: High GI risk, low CV risk, no aspirin sensitivity.
  1. 4. Q: Coxib contraindication? A: Recent CV event, severe heart failure, sulphonamide allergy (celecoxib), eGFR<15.
  1. 5. Q: Coxib + aspirin? A: Loses GI advantage; need PPI cover.

▸ 9.2.03 Nsaids Traditional Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Non-Opioid Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs) inhibit cyclo-oxygenase (COX-1, COX-2), reducing prostaglandin synthesis. Used for mild-moderate pain, especially with inflammation (bone metastases, soft tissue, post-op, RA, OA, dysmenorrhoea). Examples: ibuprofen, naproxen, diclofenac, indomethacin, ketorolac, aspirin. Side effects: GI (ulcer, bleed), renal (AKI, interstitial nephritis, papillary necrosis), CV (MI, stroke — diclofenac highest; withdrawn in UK), bronchospasm (aspirin-exacerbated respiratory disease), platelet dysfunction. Avoid in third trimester (premature closure of ductus arteriosus).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Traditional NSAIDs (ibuprofen, diclofenac, naproxen) inhibit COX-1 and COX-2, reducing prostaglandin synthesis. Anti-inflammatory, analgesic, antipyretic. Clinical features: effective for inflammatory pain (bone metastases, arthritis, soft tissue), but significant side effects: GI bleeding/ulceration (COX-1), renal impairment (prostaglandin-mediated renal perfusion), hypertension, fluid retention, heart failure exacerbation, platelet dysfunction (bleeding), bronchospasm (NSAID-exacerbated respiratory disease NERD in asthma/nasal polyps/aspirin sensitivity), pregnancy (avoid in 3rd trimester — ductus arteriosus, oligohydramnios, miscarriage).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Baseline renal (creatinine, eGFR), LFT, FBC (if chronic use, bleeding risk), BP, weight (fluid retention). HbA1c if diabetic. Consider H. pylori testing if long-term. Avoid in: peptic ulcer disease (with PPI cover), CKD stage 4-5, severe heart failure, 3rd trimester pregnancy, aspirin-exacerbated respiratory disease, peri-operative (bleeding risk), concurrent anticoagulation (high bleed risk).

2. Management

Use lowest effective dose for shortest time. Add PPI (omeprazole, lansoprazole) for GI protection in at-risk patients (age >65, prior PUD, concurrent steroids, anticoagulants). Ibuprofen 400mg TDS (least GI/CV), naproxen 500mg BD (similar CV), diclofenac 50mg TDS (highest CV). Topical diclofenac preferred for localised pain. Switch to COX-2 if GI risk dominant. Regular monitoring.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
MechanismCOX-1/COX-2 inhibition; reduce prostaglandins
ExamplesIbuprofen, naproxen, diclofenac, indomethacin
UseMild-moderate pain, inflammation, bone pain
GIUlcer, bleed (PPI cover)
RenalAKI, papillary necrosis, interstitial nephritis
CVMI, stroke (diclofenac highest, withdrawn)
RespiratoryAERD (aspirin-exacerbated respiratory disease)
PlateletReversible inhibition (aspirin irreversible)
PregnancyAvoid 3rd trimester (ductus arteriosus)
DrugsWarfarin (bleed); ACEi/ARB (renal); lithium (toxicity)

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
ParacetamolNo anti-inflammatory; safer GI
COX-2 inhibitorsLess GI risk, more CV risk
Aspirin (low dose)Antiplatelet; not for analgesia

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Triple-whammy for renal risk? A: ACEi/ARB + diuretic + NSAID (or dehydration). Avoid combination.
  1. 2. Q: NSAID with lowest CV risk? A: Naproxen (ibuprofen also low if dose ≤1200mg/day).
  1. 3. Q: NSAID with lowest GI risk? A: Ibuprofen; COX-2 inhibitors lowest but increase CV risk.
  1. 4. Q: Why avoid NSAIDs in 3rd trimester? A: Premature closure of ductus arteriosus, oligohydramnios, miscarriage, prolonged labour.
  1. 5. Q: Triple therapy for H. pylori in NSAID user? A: PPI + amoxicillin + clarithromycin (or metronidazole if penicillin-allergic).

▸ 9.2.04 Paracetamol Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Non-Opioid Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Paracetamol (acetaminophen) is a non-opioid analgesic and antipyretic. Mechanism: weak central COX inhibition (COX-1, COX-2), endocannabinoid system activation, TRPA1 ion channel inhibition. Effective for mild-moderate pain and fever. Dose: 500-1000mg q4-6h, max 4g/day (2g in liver disease, alcohol). Safe in pregnancy, paediatric, GI/renal/CV (vs NSAIDs). Hepatotoxicity in overdose: hepatocellular necrosis via toxic metabolite NAPQI (glucuronidation/sulfation exhausted); antidote: N-acetylcysteine (NAC) within 8-10h.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Paracetamol (acetaminophen) is a non-opioid analgesic and antipyretic, used for mild-moderate pain and as adjunct in WHO ladder. Mechanism: central COX inhibition (COX-3, less peripheral), serotonergic pathways, endocannabinoid reuptake inhibition. Dose: 500-1000mg q4-6h, max 4g/day (UK), 3g/day if frail/elderly/hepatic disease. Bioavailability ~70%. Onset 30-60min, duration 4-6h. Safe in pregnancy, children, hepatic metabolism (CYP2E1, glucuronidation).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

LFT (baseline in chronic use, suspected toxicity), INR (toxicity), paracetamol level at 4h post-ingestion (Rumack-Matthew nomogram for toxicity). Safe at therapeutic doses. No monitoring needed short-term.

2. Management

First-line mild-moderate pain, adjuvant to opioids (spares opioid dose, reduces opioid side effects), antipyretic, safe in pregnancy. Caution: chronic alcohol use (induces CYP2E1, toxic metabolite NAPQI accumulates), malnutrition, dehydration, hepatic disease. Toxicity: nausea/vomiting at 24h, RUQ pain at 48h, hepatic failure at 72-96h; treat with N-acetylcysteine (NAC) within 8-10h.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
MechanismCentral COX inhibition, endocannabinoid, TRPA1
Dose500-1000mg q4-6h, max 4g/day
PregnancySafe
PaediatricSafe; 15mg/kg q4-6h
CV/GI/renalSafe (vs NSAID)
OverdoseHepatocellular necrosis; NAPQI metabolite
NACN-acetylcysteine within 8-10h; 4-hourly regime
Mechanism ODGlucuronidation/sulfation saturated; CYP2E1 → NAPQI → GSH depleted
ParvolexIV NAC
RiskDose-related; reduced in liver disease, malnutrition, alcohol

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
NSAIDsAnti-inflammatory; more GI/renal/CV side effects
OpioidsStronger but more side effects; constipation, N&V, RD

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Max paracetamol dose? A: 4g/day (UK), 3g/day in frail/elderly/hepatic disease.
  1. 2. Q: Mechanism? A: Central COX inhibition (COX-3), serotonergic, endocannabinoid.
  1. 3. Q: Antidote for toxicity? A: N-acetylcysteine (NAC), most effective within 8-10h of overdose.
  1. 4. Q: Safe in pregnancy? A: Yes (category A).
  1. 5. Q: Toxic metabolite? A: NAPQI (N-acetyl-p-benzoquinone imine), via CYP2E1, accumulates in overdose and glutathione depletion.

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.2.05 Topical Analgesics Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Non-Opioid Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Topical analgesics are applied directly to the skin for localised pain, avoiding systemic side effects. Classes: topical NSAIDs (diclofenac gel, ibuprofen gel — for soft tissue/joint pain), topical capsaicin (0.025-0.075% cream, 8% patch for PHN — depletes substance P), topical lidocaine (5% patch/cream for PHN, neuropathic), topical salicylates (methyl salicylate). Useful for osteoarthritis (knee, hand), neuropathic pain (PHN), and patients who can't tolerate systemic NSAIDs.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Topical analgesics: capsaicin (TRPV1 agonist, depletes substance P, effective for post-herpetic neuralgia, diabetic neuropathy, osteoarthritis; 0.025% cream BD-TDS, 8% patch single application); lidocaine 5% patch (sodium channel blocker, neuropathic pain, especially localised; max 3 patches 12h/day); topical NSAIDs (diclofenac 1-3% gel, ibuprofen gel; effective for soft tissue/joint, less systemic side effects). Useful when systemic NSAIDs contraindicated (CKD, GI risk, elderly), or in localised neuropathic pain.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Localised pain assessment, photosensitivity, pre-application skin integrity. No specific investigations. EMLA/lidocaine toxicity in overdose.

2. Management

Apply to intact skin, avoid broken skin, eyes, mucosa. Capsaicin: pre-treat with topical anaesthetic; 8% patch requires pre-treatment due to burning. Lidocaine 5% patch: max 3 patches 12h/day, no other local anaesthetic, caution with antiarrhythmic drugs. Topical NSAID: thin layer, avoid occlusion.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
Topical NSAIDDiclofenac, ibuprofen gel; OA, soft tissue
CapsaicinSubstance P depletion; PHN 8% patch (NGX-4010)
Lidocaine 5%Patch, cream; PHN, neuropathic
SalicylatesMethyl salicylate; counter-irritant
SystemicMinimal systemic absorption
UseOA (knee, hand), PHN, localised neuropathic
Side effectsLocal: burning (capsaicin), rash, erythema

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Capsaicin vs lidocaineCapsaicin = TRPV1, substance P depletion; lidocaine = Na+ channel blocker
Topical vs systemic NSAIDLess systemic effects; localised only

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Capsaicin mechanism? A: TRPV1 agonist, depletes substance P from sensory neurons.
  1. 2. Q: Capsaicin 8% patch indication? A: Post-herpetic neuralgia; single application 60min; needs pre-treatment.
  1. 3. Q: Lidocaine 5% patch indication? A: Localised neuropathic pain (PHN); max 3 patches 12h/day.
  1. 4. Q: Topical NSAID vs systemic? A: Less systemic side effects, effective for localised soft tissue pain.
  1. 5. Q: Common side effect of capsaicin? A: Burning sensation on application (TRPV1 activation); reduced with repeated use.

▸ 9.3 Opioid Analgesics 12
▸ 9.3.01 Alfentanil Full

Related: [[../../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

[!info] Davidson alignment: Chapter 8: Pain and Palliative Care (24th Edition, Colvin, Fallon) — Opioid Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Alfentanil is a short-acting, potent synthetic opioid (μ-agonist), 10-25x more potent than morphine IV. Rapid onset (1-2 min IV, 5-10 min sublingual/buccal), short duration (10-15 min IV, 30-60 min sublingual). Available: IV (anesthesia), sublingual/buccal (Rapinyl — breakthrough cancer pain). Similar to fentanyl but shorter half-life. Side effects: similar to opioids (N&V, constipation, sedation, respiratory depression, pruritus). Used for breakthrough cancer pain (sublingual/buccal) and procedural analgesia/anesthesia (IV). Rapid onset makes it useful for incident and breakthrough pain.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Alfentanil is a short-acting synthetic opioid (μ-agonist), 10-25x more potent than IV morphine, used for breakthrough cancer pain (sublingual/buccal) and procedural analgesia. Rapid onset (1-2 min IV, 5-10 min sublingual/buccal) and short duration (10-15 min IV, 30-60 min sublingual) make it useful for incident and breakthrough pain. Side effects: N&V, constipation (chronic use), sedation, respiratory depression (dose-related), pruritus, chest wall rigidity (very high dose), bradycardia, hypotension. UK: Rapinyl (sublingual) approved for breakthrough cancer pain in opioid-tolerant patients (≥60mg oral morphine/day).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Pain assessment, opioid tolerance check, baseline pain scores, breakthrough frequency and characteristics, sedation scale, respiratory rate, drug review (interactions with CYP3A4 inhibitors — azoles, macrolides, protease inhibitors — increase levels).

2. Management

Indication: breakthrough cancer pain in opioid-tolerant. Dose: titrate from 100mcg sublingual/buccal, increase per breakthrough episode; max per episode (typically 1-2 doses, 1+ hour apart). Avoid with CYP3A4 inhibitors. Side effect management: laxative, antiemetic, sedation monitoring, naloxone for overdose. Withdraw gradually after chronic use.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ClassShort-acting opioid (μ-agonist)
Potency10-25x morphine IV
OnsetIV 1-2 min, sublingual 5-10 min
DurationIV 10-15 min, sublingual 30-60 min
IndicationsBreakthrough cancer pain (sublingual/buccal), procedural analgesia, anesthesia
Vs fentanylShorter, faster onset; less potent than fentanyl
EquianalgesiaIV 0.5-1mg ≈ IV morphine 10mg
Side effectsN&V, constipation, sedation, respiratory depression, pruritus
Opioid-tolerantRequired for breakthrough (≥60mg oral morphine/day)
DoseTitrate from 100mcg sublingual/buccal, max per breakthrough episode

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
FentanylLonger, more potent
MorphineLonger onset, oral/IV/SC/IM
SufentanilMore potent, anaesthesia

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Onset of alfentanil? A: IV 1-2 min; sublingual/buccal 5-10 min.
  1. 2. Q: Opioid tolerance for breakthrough? A: ≥60mg oral morphine/day for at least 1 week.
  1. 3. Q: Vs fentanyl? A: Shorter half-life, faster onset, less potent (10-25x morphine vs fentanyl 100x morphine).
  1. 4. Q: Why used for breakthrough? A: Rapid onset, short duration — ideal for breakthrough which is typically rapid onset, short-lived.
  1. 5. Q: Drug interactions? A: CYP3A4 inhibitors (azoles, macrolides, protease inhibitors) increase levels; benzodiazepines increase sedation.

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.3.02 Buprenorphine Transdermal Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Opioid Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Buprenorphine is a partial μ-agonist (with ceiling effect for respiratory depression) and κ-antagonist. Transdermal patch q7d. Ceiling for respiratory depression (analgesic ceiling debated). Available: sublingual (Temgesic, Subutex), patch (Butrans, Transtec), buccal film (Belbuca), implant. Equianalgesia: patch 5-20 mcg/hr = 10-35mg oral morphine/24h. Renally safe (hepatic metabolism, mostly faecal). Useful in elderly, renal impairment, opioid rotation. Less constipation.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Buprenorphine is a partial μ-agonist and κ-antagonist, available as transdermal patch (Butec, BuTrans, Transtec 5/10/15/20/35/52.5/70 mcg/hr, weekly or 96h depending on brand), sublingual (Subutex, Suboxone with naloxone for addiction), injection. Ceiling effect for respiratory depression at higher doses (analgesic ceiling controversial). Long duration (96h patch, 7-day patch). Safe in renal failure (no active metabolites, hepatic clearance).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Pain assessment (opioid-tolerant), renal function (safe), liver function (metabolised by CYP3A4; caution with inhibitors), respiratory, sedation, patch site.

2. Management

Transdermal: opioid-tolerant ≥80-120mg PO morphine/day for 7-day patch; convert 0.8-1 mcg/hr = 30mg PO morphine/day. Apply on clean, hairless skin, rotate site. Once weekly or every 96h depending on brand. Breakthrough: 1/6 of total daily dose as IR opioid. May precipitate withdrawal if switched from full agonist in opioid-tolerant (low dose — partial agonist effect).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ClassPartial μ-agonist, κ-antagonist
TransdermalPatch q7d
CeilingRD ceiling effect (analgesic debated)
Equianalgesia5-20 mcg/hr = 10-35mg oral morphine/24h
Renally safeHepatic metabolism
FormsPatch, sublingual, buccal, implant
UseElderly, renal impairment
Side effectsLess constipation, less respiratory depression

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Fentanyl patchFull agonist, every 72h, more potent
MorphineFull agonist, oral/SC/IV

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Buprenorphine classification? A: Partial μ-agonist + κ-antagonist.
  1. 2. Q: Why preferred in renal failure? A: No active metabolites, hepatic clearance, safe.
  1. 3. Q: Patch frequency? A: Every 96h (BuTrans) or 7 days (BuTrans weekly).
  1. 4. Q: Ceiling for analgesia? A: Controversial; clear ceiling for respiratory depression at high doses.
  1. 5. Q: When NOT to use? A: Acute severe pain, severe respiratory depression, severe asthma, concurrent MAOI.

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

  5. A — Less constipation, less respiratory depression

▸ 9.3.03 Codeine Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Opioid Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Codeine is a weak opioid (μ-receptor agonist prodrug) metabolised by CYP2D6 to morphine (10% of dose). Used for mild-moderate pain (WHO step 2). Dose: 30-60mg q4h PO (max 240mg/day). Variable efficacy due to CYP2D6 polymorphism: poor metabolisers (5-10%) have no analgesia; ultrarapid metabolisers (1-2% European, 10% North African) may have toxic morphine levels (respiratory depression, death — FDA boxed warning in children, lactation). Often combined with paracetamol (co-codamol 8/500, 30/500).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Codeine is a weak opioid (WHO step 2) for mild-moderate pain. Prodrug — metabolised by CYP2D6 to morphine (5-10% by O-demethylation). Equianalgesia: codeine 60mg PO = morphine 6mg PO (10:1). Side effects: constipation, N&V, sedation. CYP2D6 polymorphism: poor metabolisers (5-10% Caucasians) — no analgesia; ultra-rapid metabolisers (5% Caucasian, higher in Middle East) — morphine toxicity, life-threatening in children. Limited by ceiling effect; combination with paracetamol (co-codamol 8/500, 30/500).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Pain assessment. No routine monitoring. CYP2D6 genotype not routinely tested but consider in poor/ultra-rapid metabolisers or unexplained response.

2. Management

Co-codamol 30/500 (codeine 30mg + paracetamol 500mg) 1-2 tabs q6h. Maximum 8 tablets in 24h (limit paracetamol). Avoid codeine in children <12 (breathing problems), post-tonsillectomy, lactating (morphine in breast milk), renal failure. Use with caution in elderly. Switch to morphine if inadequate response.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ClassWeak opioid, prodrug
MechanismCYP2D6 → morphine (10% of dose)
Dose30-60mg q4h PO, max 240mg/day
MetabolismCYP2D6 polymorphism
Poor metabolisers5-10% — no analgesia
Ultrarapid1-2% European, 10% North African — toxic
CombinationCo-codamol 8/500, 30/500
Side effectsConstipation, N&V, sedation

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Tramadolμ-agonist + SNRI; less constipation
MorphineStrong opioid; no ceiling

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Codeine mechanism? A: Prodrug metabolised by CYP2D6 to morphine (~10% of dose).
  1. 2. Q: CYP2D6 polymorphism? A: Poor metabolisers (5-10% Caucasians) — no analgesia; ultra-rapid metabolisers (5%) — morphine toxicity.
  1. 3. Q: Why avoid in children? A: Risk of life-threatening respiratory depression (especially ultra-rapid metabolisers).
  1. 4. Q: Co-codamol max dose? A: 8 tablets/24h (limits paracetamol 4g).
  1. 5. Q: Equianalgesia codeine:morphine? A: 10:1 (60mg codeine = 6mg morphine).

▸ 9.3.04 Fentanyl Transdermal And Other Routes Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Opioid Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Fentanyl is a strong synthetic opioid (μ-agonist), 50-100x more potent than morphine. Available: transdermal patch (q72h, for stable pain), sublingual (Abstral, Recivit), buccal (Effentora), intranasal (Instanyl, PecFent), lozenge (Actiq), SC, IV, epidural, intrathecal. Renally safe (no active metabolites). Patches: 12-25 mcg/hr for 30-60mg oral morphine/24h; takes 24-72h to reach steady state. For stable pain only (not for opioid titration). Less constipation, but may need higher breakthrough doses. Heat increases absorption (fever, hot bath).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Fentanyl is a strong opioid (WHO step 3) with transdermal, buccal, sublingual, intranasal, SC, IV formulations. Transdermal patch (Durogesic DTrans, generic): 12.5/25/50/75/100 mcg/hr, applied every 72h, takes 24-72h to reach steady state, lasts 72h after removal (subcutaneous depot). Buccal/sublingual (Effentora, Abstral, Actiq lozenge) for breakthrough cancer pain (rapid onset 5-15 min). Highly lipophilic, 100x more potent than morphine. No active metabolites, no histamine, safe in renal failure.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Pain assessment (must be opioid-tolerant before transdermal, ≥60mg oral morphine/day for at least 1 week), renal function (safer), liver function (caution in severe), temperature (fever increases absorption), site of patch (rotate, avoid damaged skin, hairless, clean), respiratory rate, sedation.

2. Management

Transdermal: opioid-tolerant ≥60mg PO morphine/day; convert using 12.5mcg/hr = 30-60mg PO morphine/day (e.g., 25mcg/hr = 60-90mg, 50mcg/hr = 120-180mg). Apply on dry, hairless skin (chest, back, upper arm); rotate site every 72h. Breakthrough: 1/6 of total daily oral morphine equivalent as IR opioid. Buccal/sublingual for breakthrough: titration starting low. Heat increases absorption (fever, hot bath, heating pad).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ClassStrong synthetic opioid (μ agonist); 50-100x morphine
TransdermalPatch q72h, stable pain only
Equianalgesia12-25 mcg/hr = 30-60mg oral morphine/24h
Onset patch24-72h to steady state
Renally safeNo active metabolites
Side effectsLess constipation, more dry mouth
Other routesSublingual, buccal, intranasal, IV, IT
BreakthroughSublingual/buccal/intranasal
HeatFever, hot bath, heating pad — ↑ absorption (toxicity)

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
MorphineMore histamine, active metabolites
Buprenorphine patchLonger, but partial agonist

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: When to use transdermal fentanyl? A: Opioid-tolerant, stable pain, oral not possible (vomiting, dysphagia), renal failure, compliance.
  1. 2. Q: Patch onset/duration? A: Onset 24-72h (subcutaneous depot); apply every 72h; lasts 72h after removal.
  1. 3. Q: Breakthrough on fentanyl patch? A: IR opioid, ~1/6 of total daily dose, q1h prn (separate from patch).
  1. 4. Q: Why is fentanyl renally safe? A: No active metabolites (metabolised in liver by CYP3A4 to inactive metabolites).
  1. 5. Q: Why avoid in opioid-naïve? A: Risk of severe respiratory depression; transdermal not titratable for acute pain.

▸ 9.3.05 Hydromorphone Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Opioid Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Hydromorphone is a strong semi-synthetic opioid agonist, 5-7x more potent than oral morphine (oral morphine 30mg = oral hydromorphone 5-6mg = parenteral hydromorphone 1.5-2mg), alternative strong opioid (WHO step 3) for cancer pain. Pure μ-receptor agonist. Available: oral immediate release (Palladone IR), oral modified release (Palladone SR), injection (SC, IV, IM), suppositories. Indications: alternative to morphine (alternative side effects, renal failure — no active metabolites, better tolerated), opioid rotation when morphine not tolerated (less histamine, less hallucinations, less nausea for some). Cautions: similar to morphine — opioid-naïve caution, history of substance use, elderly. Side effects: similar to morphine (constipation, N&V, sedation, respiratory depression, pruritus), but less histamine release (less pruritus), less active metabolites (safer in renal failure). Dosing: opioid-naïve 1-2mg PO q4-6h IR; MR 4-8mg OD. Equianalgesia: PO morphine 30mg = PO hydromorphone 6mg = IV hydromorphone 2mg = SC hydromorphone 3mg.

Key clinical pearls:

  • Pure μ-agonist
  • Less histamine than morphine
  • No active metabolites (renal safe)
  • Better tolerated for some

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Hydromorphone is a strong opioid (WHO step 3) for severe cancer pain, alternative to morphine. Semi-synthetic, μ-agonist. 5-7x more potent than morphine. Available: IR (Palladone IR 1.3/2.6mg), MR (Palladone SR 2/4/8/16/24mg), injection (SC, IV, IM), suppositories. Side effects: similar to morphine, but less histamine (less pruritus), less neuroexcitatory metabolites (safer in renal failure). No active metabolites.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Pain assessment, renal function (safer than morphine, dose reduction only in severe failure), liver function, sedation, bowel function.

2. Management

Start: opioid-naïve 1.3mg q4-6h IR or 4mg 24-hourly MR. Titrate. Equianalgesia: PO morphine 30mg = PO hydromorphone 6mg = IV/SC hydromorphone 2mg. Good alternative for patients with renal impairment or morphine intolerance.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ClassStrong opioid (μ agonist)
PotentOral hydromorphone 8mg = oral morphine 30mg
Less histamineLess pruritus
RenalNo active metabolites
Dose1.3mg q4h IR (naïve)

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
MorphineLess potent, more metabolites
FentanylTransdermal, more potent, less histamine

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Hydromorphone vs morphine potency? A: 5-7x more potent (PO 6mg = PO morphine 30mg).
  1. 2. Q: Why use instead of morphine? A: No active metabolites (safer in renal failure), less histamine (less pruritus), alternative if morphine side effects.
  1. 3. Q: Available routes? A: PO (IR, MR), SC, IV, IM, PR.
  1. 4. Q: When is hydromorphone preferred? A: Renal failure, morphine intolerance (hallucinations, severe N&V), high opioid doses.
  1. 5. Q: Equianalgesia? A: PO morphine 30mg = PO hydromorphone 6mg (5:1) = IV/SC hydromorphone 2mg (15:1).

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.3.06 Methadone Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Opioid Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Methadone is a synthetic opioid (μ-agonist) with additional NMDA-receptor antagonist and serotonin/NA reuptake inhibition. Useful for neuropathic cancer pain, opioid rotation, methadone maintenance (addiction). Dose: 2.5-5mg q8-12h PO. Equianalgesia variable — depends on prior opioid dose. Long half-life (15-60h); complex pharmacokinetics; risk of QT prolongation, serotonin syndrome. Multiple drug interactions via CYP3A4, CYP2B6. Prescribe only by specialists (palliative care, pain, addiction).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Methadone is a strong synthetic opioid (WHO step 3) for severe cancer pain, particularly useful in neuropathic pain and renal failure. μ-agonist + NMDA antagonist + serotonin/NA reuptake inhibition. Long, variable half-life (8-80h, average 24h), slow onset, complex pharmacokinetics. Equianalgesic ratio variable, conversion complex. No active metabolites, safe in renal failure, low cost. Issues: QT prolongation, drug interactions (CYP3A4, CYP2B6, CYP2D6), accumulates in hepatic impairment.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

ECG (QTc) before and during treatment (withhold if QTc >500ms), drug interactions (CYP3A4 inhibitors — azoles, macrolides, SSRIs, grapefruit), renal function, liver function, pain assessment, sedation, bowel function. Specialist supervision recommended for initiation.

2. Management

Specialist use. Conversion from another opioid: complex, requires specialist guidance, consider overlap with reduced dose of previous opioid. Titrate slowly (long half-life, accumulation). QT monitoring. Drug interactions common. Useful in neuropathic pain (NMDA antagonist), renal failure. Available: oral (Methadose, Physeptone), injection.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ClassSynthetic opioid (μ) + NMDA antagonist + SNRI
UseNeuropathic pain, opioid rotation, addiction
Half-life15-60h (long)
QTcProlongation; ECG monitoring
Drug interactionsCYP3A4, CYP2B6 (multiple)
EquianalgesiaVariable (specialist prescribing)
SerotoninRisk of serotonin syndrome
SpecialistPalliative care, pain, addiction only

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
MorphineShorter half-life, easier titration
FentanylRenally safe, simpler conversion

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Why use methadone in neuropathic pain? A: NMDA antagonist + μ-agonist; multiple mechanisms.
  1. 2. Q: Half-life? A: Variable 8-80h (average 24h); accumulation risk.
  1. 3. Q: Why ECG before? A: QT prolongation (dose-dependent, >500ms withhold).
  1. 4. Q: Equianalgesia vs morphine? A: Variable (3:1 to 20:1 depending on dose and patient), not straightforward.
  1. 5. Q: Drug interactions? A: CYP3A4 inhibitors (azoles, macrolides, grapefruit), CYP2B6 (efavirenz, rifampicin), increase or decrease levels.

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.3.07 Morphine Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Opioid Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Morphine is the gold-standard strong opioid for moderate-severe cancer pain (WHO step 3). Pure μ-receptor agonist. Available: oral immediate release (Sevredol, Oramorph), oral modified release (MST Continus, Zomorph), SC, IV, IM, rectal, intrathecal. Dose: 5-10mg q4h IR (opioid-naïve); MR 30mg BD (typical starting total daily dose). Equianalgesia: oral 30mg = SC 15mg = IV 10mg. Active metabolites (M3G, M6G) accumulate in renal failure (use fentanyl/buprenorphine instead). Side effects: constipation, N&V, sedation, respiratory depression (late), pruritus, OIH.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Morphine is the gold-standard strong opioid (WHO step 3) for cancer pain and severe chronic pain. Pure μ-agonist. Available: oral IR (Sevredol, Oramorph 5/10/20mg/1mL), oral MR (MST Continus 5/10/15/30/60/100/200mg, Zomorph 10/30/60/100/200mg, both 12-hourly), SC, IV, IM, PR, intrathecal, epidural. Bioavailability PO 30%, SC 100%, IV 100%. Metabolites: M3G (neuroexcitatory), M6G (active, 2x potency), accumulate in renal failure. Equianalgesia: PO 30mg = SC 15mg = IV 10mg. Side effects: constipation, N&V, sedation, respiratory depression (late sign), pruritus, urinary retention, OIH.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Pain assessment, sedation score, respiratory rate, bowel function, baseline renal (switch to fentanyl/buprenorphine if eGFR<30), liver function. No routine monitoring in stable patients. Morphine levels not clinically used.

2. Management

Start: opioid-naïve 5-10mg PO q4h IR or 30mg MR 12-hourly; switch from weak opioid (codeine 240mg/day = morphine 30mg/day). Titrate by 25-50% every 24-48h. Co-prescribe stimulant laxative (senna) + softener (docusate). Antiemetic prn first 1-2 weeks. Breakthrough: 1/6 of total daily dose, q1h prn. Side effect management: constipation (laxatives), N&V (antiemetic), sedation (reduce dose), respiratory depression (naloxone 3-5mcg IV, repeat).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ClassStrong opioid (μ-agonist)
Gold standardWHO step 3, cancer pain
FormulationsIR (Sevredol, Oramorph), MR (MST, Zomorph), SC, IV, IM, PR, IT
Dose5-10mg q4h IR (naïve); MR 30mg BD
EquianalgesiaPO 30mg = SC 15mg = IV 10mg
MetabolitesM3G, M6G; accumulate in renal failure
Renal failureSwitch to fentanyl/buprenorphine
Side effectsConstipation, N&V, sedation, RD, pruritus, OIH

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
OxycodoneLess histamine, dual μ + κ
FentanylRenally safe, transdermal
HydromorphoneMore potent, renally safer

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Equianalgesia PO:SC:IV for morphine? A: 30:15:10 mg (3:1.5:1).
  1. 2. Q: Active metabolites of morphine? A: M6G (active, 2x potency); M3G (neuroexcitatory); both accumulate in renal failure.
  1. 3. Q: Why avoid in renal failure? A: M3G/M6G accumulation → prolonged effect, neuroexcitatory, N&V; switch to fentanyl or buprenorphine.
  1. 4. Q: Breakthrough dose? A: 1/6 of total daily dose, q1h prn.
  1. 5. Q: First sign of opioid toxicity? A: Sedation, then respiratory depression (late).

▸ 9.3.09 Opioid Pharmacology Overview Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Opioid Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Opioids bind to opioid receptors (μ, δ, κ) for analgesia. Full agonists: morphine, oxycodone, hydromorphone, fentanyl, methadone, codeine (prodrug), tramadol. Partial agonists: buprenorphine (μ partial, κ antagonist). Mixed agonist-antagonist: pentazocine, nalbuphine, butorphanol. Side effects mediated by μ-receptors: constipation (gut), N&V (CTZ), sedation (CNS), respiratory depression (brainstem), pruritus (μ in mast cells), miosis (Edinger-Westphal nucleus). Tolerance, dependence (physical), addiction (psychological — different).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Opioids bind to μ (mu), κ (kappa), δ (delta) opioid receptors in CNS, peripheral nerves, GI. μ-receptor: analgesia, sedation, euphoria, respiratory depression, miosis, constipation, pruritus, N&V. κ: analgesia, sedation, diuresis, dysphoria. δ: analgesia, anxiolysis. Receptor types: full agonist (morphine, oxycodone, fentanyl, methadone, codeine, hydromorphone, tramadol, tapentadol), partial agonist (buprenorphine — ceiling effect for respiratory depression at higher doses), mixed agonist-antagonist (pentazocine, nalbuphine — avoid with full agonist due to withdrawal).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Pain assessment, baseline renal function (morphine metabolites accumulate in renal failure — switch to fentanyl/buprenorphine), liver function (metabolites in liver failure), respiratory rate, sedation score (Pasero Opioid-Induced Sedation Scale, POSS), bowel function. No routine blood monitoring needed for stable opioid. ECG before methadone (QT prolongation).

2. Management

WHO ladder: mild pain = paracetamol ± NSAID; moderate = weak opioid (codeine, tramadol); severe = strong opioid (morphine, oxycodone, fentanyl, buprenorphine). Always co-prescribe laxative (especially for constipation) and antiemetic (for N&V first 1-2 weeks). Regular + breakthrough doses. Switch to alternative opioid if inadequate response or side effects (rotation). Naloxone for overdose (3-5mcg IV, repeat).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
Receptorsμ (analgesia, RD, constipation, N&V), δ, κ
Full agonistMorphine, oxycodone, hydromorphone, fentanyl, methadone
Partial agonistBuprenorphine
MixedPentazocine, nalbuphine, butorphanol
AntagonistNaloxone (IV), naltrexone (PO)
Side effectsConstipation, N&V, sedation, RD, pruritus, miosis
ToleranceRight shift of dose-response
DependencePhysical (withdrawal on stop)
AddictionPsychological, compulsive, harm

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Opioid agonists (full)Morphine, oxycodone, fentanyl
Partial agonistBuprenorphine
Mixed agonist-antagonistPentazocine, nalbuphine

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Three opioid receptors? A: μ (mu, analgesia, RD, constipation), κ (kappa, analgesia, sedation, dysphoria), δ (delta, analgesia, mood).
  1. 2. Q: Weak vs strong opioid? A: Weak: codeine, tramadol (ceiling effect); strong: morphine, oxycodone, fentanyl, buprenorphine.
  1. 3. Q: Buprenorphine classification? A: Partial agonist; ceiling for respiratory depression but not for analgesia (controversial at high doses).
  1. 4. Q: Why switch opioid in renal failure? A: Morphine metabolites (M3G, M6G) accumulate; switch to fentanyl or buprenorphine.
  1. 5. Q: Opioid-induced hyperalgesia vs tolerance? A: OIH = increased pain sensitivity to opioid, treated by reducing dose or switching; tolerance = decreased effect of same dose, treated by increasing dose.

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.3.10 Oxycodone Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Opioid Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Oxycodone is a strong opioid (μ- and κ-receptor agonist) for moderate-severe cancer pain. Alternative to morphine. Available: IR (Oxynorm), MR (OxyContin). Dose: 5mg q4-6h IR (naïve); MR 10mg BD. Equianalgesia: oral oxycodone 20mg = oral morphine 30mg (1.5:1). Less histamine release than morphine (less pruritus). Also available combined with naloxone (Targinact) to reduce opioid-induced constipation (naloxone local gut action, not absorbed).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Oxycodone is a strong opioid (WHO step 3) for moderate-severe cancer pain. Semi-synthetic, dual μ-agonist and κ-agonist. Bioavailability 60-87% (higher than morphine). Available: IR (Oxynorm 5/10/20mg, capsule), MR (Oxycontin 5/10/20/40/80/120mg, 12-hourly). Side effects: similar to morphine — constipation, N&V, sedation, respiratory depression, pruritus; less histamine (less pruritus). Metabolites: oxymorphone (active, renally excreted), noroxycodone. Caution in renal/hepatic impairment. Potency: oral oxycodone 1.5-2x oral morphine.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Pain assessment, renal function (active metabolites), liver function, sedation, bowel function.

2. Management

Start: opioid-naïve 5mg q4h IR or 10mg 12-hourly MR. Titrate by 25-50%. Equianalgesia: oxycodone PO 20mg = morphine PO 30mg (1:1.5). Often used as first-line alternative to morphine (less histamine, fewer hallucinations). Available in IR + MR combination for breakthrough.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ClassStrong opioid (μ + κ agonist)
FormulationsIR (Oxynorm), MR (OxyContin), SC, IV
Dose5mg q4-6h IR (naïve)
EquianalgesiaPO oxycodone 20mg = PO morphine 30mg
Less histamineLess pruritus than morphine
TarginactCombined with naloxone (prolonged release) for constipation

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
MorphineStandard strong opioid
HydromorphoneMore potent, less histamine

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Oxycodone vs morphine potency? A: Oxycodone ~1.5-2x more potent (PO 20mg = PO morphine 30mg).
  1. 2. Q: Why prefer over morphine? A: Less histamine (less pruritus), less neuroexcitatory metabolites, dual μ + κ.
  1. 3. Q: Bioavailability? A: 60-87% (higher than morphine ~30%).
  1. 4. Q: Active metabolite? A: Oxymorphone.
  1. 5. Q: Can convert directly to morphine? A: Yes, 1.5:1 (10mg oxycodone = 15mg morphine).

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.3.11 Tapentadol Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Opioid Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Tapentadol is a unique dual-mechanism analgesic: μ-opioid receptor agonist (MOR, 18x weaker than morphine) AND noradrenaline reuptake inhibitor (NRI), giving analgesia for both nociceptive and neuropathic pain. Available: oral immediate release (Palexia IR 50/75/100mg), oral modified release (Palexia SR 50-250mg BD). Indications: moderate-severe acute and chronic pain, including neuropathic pain (diabetic neuropathy, low back pain with neuropathic component). Dose: IR 50-100mg q4-6h (max 600mg/day); SR 50-250mg BD. Side effects: similar to opioids (N&V, constipation, dizziness, somnolence) but less than equianalgesic morphine; lower abuse potential than pure opioids; serotonin syndrome risk (with SSRIs, SNRIs, MAOIs, TCAs). Equianalgesia: 50mg tapentadol ≈ 50mg tramadol ≈ 10mg morphine (approximate).

Key clinical pearls:

  • Dual mechanism: MOR + NRI
  • Neuropathic + nociceptive
  • Lower abuse potential
  • Serotonin syndrome risk

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Tapentadol is a strong opioid (also approved for moderate-severe pain) with dual mechanism: μ-agonist (18x weaker than morphine) + noradrenaline reuptake inhibitor (NRI). Effective for nociceptive and neuropathic pain. Available: IR (Palexia IR 50/75/100mg, q4-6h, max 600mg/day), MR (Palexia SR 50/75/100/150/200/250mg, BD). Side effects: N&V, constipation, dizziness, somnolence (less than equianalgesic morphine), lower abuse potential, less respiratory depression. Serotonin syndrome risk (with SSRIs, SNRIs, TCAs, MAOIs, triptans, linezolid).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Pain assessment, renal function (reduce dose in severe impairment), liver function (avoid in severe), monitoring for serotonin syndrome, no ECG required (unlike methadone).

2. Management

Start IR 50mg q4-6h, max 600mg/day; MR 50mg BD, titrate to max 250mg BD. Equianalgesia: tapentadol 50mg ≈ tramadol 50mg ≈ morphine 10mg. Avoid with MAOIs (serotonin syndrome, severe reaction including death), SSRIs, SNRIs, TCAs (use with caution).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ClassStrong opioid (μ) + NRI
Dose50-100mg q4-6h IR; MR 50-250mg BD
UseMixed nociceptive-neuropathic
LessConstipation, N&V
AvoidSevere renal failure, asthma, MAOI

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Tramadolμ + 5HT/NA; more seizure risk
MorphinePure μ; more constipation, RD

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Tapentadol mechanism? A: μ-agonist (weak) + NRI (dual).
  1. 2. Q: Difference from tramadol? A: No serotonin effect (only NRI, no 5HT reuptake), so less seizure risk and serotonin syndrome.
  1. 3. Q: Serotonin syndrome risk? A: Yes, with SSRIs, SNRIs, TCAs, MAOIs, triptans, linezolid.
  1. 4. Q: Abuse potential vs morphine? A: Lower.
  1. 5. Q: Maximum dose? A: IR 600mg/day, MR 500mg/day (250mg BD).

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.3.12 Tramadol Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Opioid Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Tramadol is a weak opioid (μ-receptor agonist) with additional SNRI activity (serotonin and noradrenaline reuptake inhibition), making it useful for mixed and neuropathic pain. Dose: 50-100mg q6h, max 400mg/day (or 300mg in elderly). Avoid in epilepsy (lowers seizure threshold), MAOI (serotonin syndrome), and severe asthma. Often combined with paracetamol. Side effects: N&V, dizziness, seizures, serotonin syndrome with SSRIs/MAOIs. Less constipation than other opioids.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Tramadol is a weak opioid (WHO step 2) with dual mechanism: μ-agonist + serotonin and noradrenaline reuptake inhibition (SNRI). Effective for nociceptive and neuropathic pain. Equianalgesia: tramadol 100mg PO = morphine 10mg PO (10:1). Side effects: constipation (less than morphine), N&V, sedation, dizziness, seizure threshold lowering, serotonin syndrome (with SSRIs, SNRIs, MAOIs, TCAs, linezolid, triptans). Caution: renal failure (reduce dose, accumulate), hepatic failure. Available: IR (50mg), MR (100/150/200/300mg).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Pain assessment, renal function (dose reduction in eGFR<30), liver function (dose reduction in cirrhosis), monitoring for serotonin syndrome (mental status, autonomic instability, hyperreflexia, clonus).

2. Management

Start 50mg q6h IR or 100mg BD MR. Titrate by 50-100mg/day. Maximum 400mg/day (300mg in elderly). Reduce dose in renal/hepatic impairment. Avoid with SSRIs, SNRIs, TCAs, MAOIs (serotonin syndrome risk). Caution with epilepsy (lowers seizure threshold). Withdraw gradually (N&V, anxiety, sweating).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ClassWeak opioid + SNRI
Mechanismμ-agonist + serotonin/NA reuptake inhibition
Dose50-100mg q6h, max 400mg/day
UseMixed, neuropathic
AvoidEpilepsy (lowers threshold), MAOI, severe asthma
CombinationCo-tramadol 37.5/325
Side effectsN&V, dizziness, seizures, serotonin syndrome
Less constipationCompared to other opioids

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
CodeinePure prodrug, less SNRI
Tapentadolμ-agonist + NRI, no serotonin effect, less seizure risk

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Tramadol mechanism? A: μ-agonist + serotonin/noradrenaline reuptake inhibition (dual).
  1. 2. Q: Equianalgesia tramadol:morphine? A: 10:1 (100mg tramadol = 10mg morphine).
  1. 3. Q: Serotonin syndrome risk? A: Yes, with SSRIs/SNRIs/TCAs/MAOIs (excess serotonergic activity).
  1. 4. Q: Side effect vs morphine? A: Less constipation, more dizziness, seizure threshold lowering.
  1. 5. Q: Maximum dose? A: 400mg/day (300mg in elderly).

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.4 Neuropathic Pain 7
▸ 9.4.01 Central Post Stroke Pain Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Neuropathic Pain

1. 1. Definition

Central post-stroke pain (CPSP, thalamic pain syndrome, Dejerine-Roussy syndrome) is neuropathic pain arising from a CNS lesion (typically thalamic stroke, but any stroke). Onset weeks-months after stroke. Features: burning, electric, aching in the affected side, often hemibody distribution; sensory loss; allodynia, hyperalgesia. Treatment: TCAs (amitriptyline — first-line for CPSP specifically), gabapentin, pregabalin, lamotrigine; tramadol; neuromodulation (deep brain stimulation, motor cortex stimulation).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Central post-stroke pain (CPSP, thalamic pain syndrome) is neuropathic pain developing after a stroke (typically thalamic, brainstem, or other CNS lesions), in the body region corresponding to the lesion. Affects 10% of stroke patients, often months-years after. Features: burning, aching, pressing, lacerating, often with allodynia, hyperalgesia, dysaesthesia. Often associated with sensory loss (thalamic pain = Dejerine-Roussy syndrome).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

MRI brain (infarct or other lesion), correlate with pain distribution. Trial of analgesia with monitoring.

2. Management

First-line: amitriptyline 25-150mg nocte (most evidence), gabapentin, pregabalin, duloxetine. Second-line: lamotrigine, carbamazepine, fluvoxamine. Refractory: motor cortex stimulation, deep brain stimulation, spinal cord stimulation. Limited evidence for opioids.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
TypeCentral neuropathic pain after stroke
SiteThalamic (most common), but any stroke
OnsetWeeks-months after stroke
FeaturesBurning, electric, hemibody, sensory loss
First-lineAmitriptyline (TCA)
Second-lineGabapentin, pregabalin, lamotrigine
Third-lineTramadol, neuromodulation

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
SpasticityMuscle tone, not pain
Complex regional painLocalised, autonomic features
Cervical/lumbar radiculopathyPre-existing, not post-stroke

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: What is thalamic pain syndrome? A: Central post-stroke pain (Dejerine-Roussy) from thalamic infarct; contralateral burning pain with sensory loss.
  1. 2. Q: First-line treatment? A: Amitriptyline (TCA, most evidence).
  1. 3. Q: When does pain develop after stroke? A: Days to years (most within 1 year).
  1. 4. Q: Brain regions most associated? A: Thalamus, brainstem, parietal cortex, insula.
  1. 5. Q: Prevalence after stroke? A: 10% of stroke patients (up to 30% of sensory strokes).

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

  • Q: Key pharmacological/clinical feature?

▸ 9.4.02 Complex Regional Pain Syndrome Crps Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Neuropathic Pain

1. 1. Definition

Complex regional pain syndrome (CRPS, formerly reflex sympathetic dystrophy, causalgia) is a chronic neuropathic pain condition affecting a limb, often after trauma, surgery, or stroke, with disproportionate pain, sensory changes, vasomotor, sudomotor, motor/trophic changes. Two types: Type 1 (no nerve injury, was RSD) and Type 2 (definitive nerve injury, was causalgia). Budapest diagnostic criteria. Treatment: multidisciplinary — physiotherapy (graded motor imagery, mirror therapy), TCAs, gabapentin, ketamine (oral/topical/IV), bisphosphonates, sympathetic blocks, SCS.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Complex regional pain syndrome (CRPS) is a chronic neuropathic pain disorder, type 1 (no nerve injury, was reflex sympathetic dystrophy) or type 2 (with nerve injury, was causalgia). Often after trauma (fracture, sprain, surgery, stroke). Budapest criteria: persistent pain disproportionate to injury, with sensory (allodynia, hyperalgesia), vasomotor (temperature, colour asymmetry), sudomotor (oedema, sweating) abnormalities, motor/trophic (weakness, dystonia, atrophy, hair/nail changes) signs. CRPS typically affects distal limb, often unilateral.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Clinical (Budapest criteria — 4 categories, need ≥1 sign in ≥3 categories for diagnosis). Imaging: X-ray (patchy demineralisation in late), MRI (oedema, atrophy, bone marrow oedema). Bone scan: increased uptake in affected limb (early).

2. Management

Multimodal, multidisciplinary: early mobilisation (key), physiotherapy (graded exercise, desensitisation, mirror therapy), occupational therapy. Pharmacological: NSAIDs, paracetamol, gabapentin, pregabalin, amitriptyline, duloxetine, ketamine (oral or IV), bisphosphonates (especially if bone involvement), corticosteroids (early), topical capsaicin or lidocaine. Interventional: sympathetic blocks (stellate ganglion for upper limb, lumbar sympathetic for lower), spinal cord stimulation (for refractory). Psychological support.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
Type 1No nerve injury (was RSD)
Type 2Nerve injury (was causalgia)
Budapest criteriaContinuing pain disproportionate; sensory, vasomotor, sudomotor, motor/trophic
TriggerTrauma, surgery, stroke
TreatmentPhysiotherapy (graded motor imagery, mirror)
DrugsTCAs, gabapentin, ketamine, bisphosphonates
BlocksSympathetic blocks, SCS
Three-phaseAcute (warm, red), dystrophic (cool, cyanotic), atrophic

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
CellulitisInfection, fever, leukocytosis
DVTAcute, painful, swelling, Doppler
OsteomyelitisBone infection, fever, MRI
Peripheral neuropathyLength-dependent, no autonomic features

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Budapest criteria for CRPS? A: Persistent pain disproportionate to injury + sensory/vasomotor/sudomotor/motor signs (≥1 sign in ≥3 categories).
  1. 2. Q: Difference CRPS type 1 vs 2? A: Type 1: no nerve injury (was RSD); Type 2: with nerve injury (was causalgia).
  1. 3. Q: First-line treatment? A: Physiotherapy (graded, desensitisation, mirror therapy) + adjuvant analgesia.
  1. 4. Q: Sympathetic block indication? A: Refractory CRPS with sympathetic features (temperature, sweating).
  1. 5. Q: Bisphosphonates in CRPS? A: Yes, can help especially with bone oedema, allodynia, stiffness.

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features
