▸ 9.4.04 Painful Diabetic Neuropathy Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Neuropathic Pain

1. 1. Definition

Painful diabetic neuropathy (PDN) is a common complication of diabetes (affects ~25% of diabetic patients with neuropathy), typically distal symmetric sensorimotor polyneuropathy with burning, electric, pins-and-needles pain in feet (worse at night). Risk factors: poor glycaemic control, duration of diabetes, older age, smoking, alcohol. Treatment: NICE-recommended first-line: TCAs (amitriptyline), SNRIs (duloxetine — first-line in many guidelines), gabapentinoids (gabapentin, pregabalin). Prevention: optimal glycaemic control, lifestyle. Second-line: tramadol, capsaicin, lidocaine. Third-line: neuromodulation (spinal cord stimulation).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Painful diabetic neuropathy affects 30% of patients with diabetes mellitus, often as distal symmetric polyneuropathy (DSPN) with burning, shooting, electric, pins-and-needles in stocking-and-glove distribution (feet first, hands later). Worse at night. Associated with autonomic features (orthostatic hypotension, gastroparesis, bladder/bowel dysfunction), and other complications (retinopathy, nephropathy). Painful in only 10-20% of all diabetic neuropathy, but disabling. Risk factors: duration of diabetes, poor glycaemic control, obesity, smoking, hypertension.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

HbA1c (control), B12 (exclude deficiency, especially on metformin), folate, renal function (U&E), TFT, autoimmune screen, paraprotein (exclude other causes), nerve conduction studies (large fibre, axonal sensory), MRI spine (if asymmetric/radicular to exclude compression), skin biopsy (small fibre neuropathy — IENFD).

2. Management

Optimise glycaemic control (HbA1c <7%; intensive control reduces progression). First-line: duloxetine 60-120mg/day (NICE, EMA-approved for diabetic neuropathy), gabapentin, pregabalin, amitriptyline. Combination therapy if inadequate. Topical: lidocaine 5% patch, capsaicin 8% patch. Tramadol, strong opioids (third-line). Non-pharm: TENS, foot care, psychological support. Treat autonomic features (orthostatic hypotension, gastroparesis).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
Prevalence~25% of diabetic neuropathy
TypeDistal symmetric polyneuropathy
SiteFeet, ascending
FeaturesBurning, electric, pins-and-needles, worse at night
RiskPoor control, duration, age, smoking, alcohol
First-lineDuloxetine (SNRI), TCAs (amitriptyline), pregabalin
PreventionGlycaemic control, lifestyle
Second-lineTramadol, capsaicin, lidocaine

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
B12 deficiencyMCV high, dorsal column signs
Alcoholic neuropathyHistory, MCV high
CIDPMotor + sensory, demyelinating
Spinal stenosisAsymmetric, radicular

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: First-line systemic for painful diabetic neuropathy? A: Duloxetine (60-120mg/day), gabapentin, pregabalin, amitriptyline.
  1. 2. Q: Why duloxetine? A: SNRI; FDA/EMA approved; addresses both pain and depression (common comorbidity).
  1. 3. Q: Prevention? A: Intensive glycaemic control (HbA1c <7%); lifestyle; early screening.
  1. 4. Q: Autonomic features? A: Orthostatic hypotension, gastroparesis, bladder/bowel dysfunction, sweating, erectile dysfunction.
  1. 5. Q: Distinction from B12 neuropathy? A: B12: subacute combined degeneration (posterior column + corticospinal + peripheral), macrocytosis; DM: stocking-glove, no dorsal column signs, normal MCV.

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.4.05 Post Herpetic Neuralgia Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Neuropathic Pain

1. 1. Definition

Post-herpetic neuralgia (PHN) is neuropathic pain persisting ≥3 months after rash onset in herpes zoster (shingles). Risk factors: older age (>50), severe rash, severe acute pain, immunosuppression, ophthalmic zoster. Features: burning, electric, allodynia, hyperalgesia in dermatomal distribution. Prevention: zoster vaccine (Shingrix, >50y). Treatment: first-line: TCAs, gabapentin, pregabalin, topical lidocaine 5% patch, topical capsaicin 8% patch (NGX-4010, single application, highly effective); second-line: tramadol, oxycodone; third-line: neuromodulation, intrathecal steroids.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Post-herpetic neuralgia (PHN) is neuropathic pain persisting >3 months after herpes zoster rash, in the dermatome distribution (typically thoracic, trigeminal V1 — ophthalmic). Affects 10-20% of zoster patients (50% over 60). Features: burning, shooting, electric, with allodynia (light touch painful), hyperalgesia, dysaesthesia. Risk factors: older age (>50), severe prodromal/acute pain, severe rash, ophthalmic zoster, immunosuppression. Often improves over months-years but can be lifelong.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Clinical (pain in dermatome after healed zoster). No specific tests. Consider zoster PCR if atypical. May show sensory loss in affected dermatome.

2. Management

First-line: gabapentin 300mg TDS titrated to 3600mg/day, OR pregabalin 150-600mg/day, OR amitriptyline 25-150mg nocte, OR duloxetine 60-120mg/day. Topical: lidocaine 5% patch (especially for elderly, less systemic side effects), capsaicin 8% patch (single 60min application, high efficacy). Interventional: sympathetic blocks, spinal cord stimulation, intrathecal steroids. Tramadol, strong opioids (third-line, short-term trial).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
DefinitionPHN ≥3 months after zoster rash
RiskAge >50, severe rash, severe pain
FeaturesBurning, electric, allodynia, dermatomal
PreventionShingrix >50y
First-lineTCAs, gabapentin, pregabalin, lidocaine 5% patch
Capsaicin 8%NGX-4010 patch, single application
Second-lineTramadol, oxycodone
Third-lineNeuromodulation, IT steroids

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Trigeminal neuralgiaV2/V3, brief paroxysmal
Diabetic neuropathyStocking-glove, diabetes history
Spinal nerve root painDermatomal, radicular

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Definition of PHN? A: Neuropathic pain persisting >3 months after herpes zoster rash, in the dermatome distribution.
  1. 2. Q: Most effective topical for PHN? A: Lidocaine 5% patch (suitable for elderly) or capsaicin 8% patch (single 60min application).
  1. 3. Q: Risk factors for PHN? A: Older age (>50), severe acute pain, severe rash, ophthalmic zoster, immunosuppression.
  1. 4. Q: First-line systemic? A: Gabapentin, pregabalin, amitriptyline, or duloxetine (NICE).
  1. 5. Q: Prevention of PHN? A: Zoster vaccine (Shingrix >50 years) reduces risk; early antiviral (within 72h) may reduce severity; gabapentin during acute phase is being studied.

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.4.06 Spinal Cord Stimulation Scs Full

Related: [[../../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

[!info] Davidson alignment: Chapter 8: Pain and Palliative Care (24th Edition, Colvin, Fallon) — Neuropathic Pain

1. 1. Definition

Spinal cord stimulation (SCS) is an interventional neuromodulation technique for chronic refractory neuropathic pain. Implanted electrode in epidural space delivers electrical stimulation to dorsal columns, modulating pain signals. Indications: failed back surgery syndrome (FBSS, most common), CRPS, refractory neuropathic pain (peripheral, PHN, diabetic neuropathy), refractory angina, ischaemic limb pain (controversial). Patient selection: trial (temporary electrode, 1-2 weeks), then permanent implant if ≥50% pain relief. Mode: traditional (paresthesia-based), high-frequency (10kHz, paresthesia-free), burst, dorsal root ganglion (DRG). Evidence: good for FBSS and CRPS; less robust for other indications. Multidisciplinary team (pain, neurosurgery, psychology). Contraindications: untreated psychiatric disease, drug abuse, infection, anticoagulation (relative).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Spinal cord stimulation (SCS) is an interventional neuromodulation technique for chronic refractory neuropathic pain. Implanted epidural electrode delivers electrical stimulation to dorsal columns, modulating pain signals (gate control theory). Indications: failed back surgery syndrome (FBSS, most common, 70% success), complex regional pain syndrome (CRPS, 70% success), refractory neuropathic pain (PHN, diabetic neuropathy), refractory angina, ischaemic limb pain (controversial). Modes: traditional (paresthesia-based, 30-60Hz), high-frequency (10kHz, paresthesia-free), burst, dorsal root ganglion (DRG) stimulation. Patient selection: trial (temporary electrode, 1-2 weeks), then permanent implant if ≥50% pain relief. Multidisciplinary team (pain, neurosurgery, psychology).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Patient selection: trial period (1-2 weeks temporary electrode). Response threshold: ≥50% pain reduction. Psychological evaluation. Imaging: MRI spine (rule out lesion, assess epidural space). Trial outcomes: pain, function, QoL, opioid use. Permanent implant: device placement, programming, follow-up.

2. Management

Trial: temporary electrode, 1-2 weeks; ≥50% pain reduction → proceed to permanent. Permanent implant: generator (battery — rechargeable ~10 years, non-rechargeable replace), electrode (cervical, thoracic, lumbar based on pain location), programming. Modes: traditional (paresthesia required), high-frequency 10kHz (paresthesia-free), burst, DRG. Multidisciplinary follow-up. Complications: lead migration (10-20%), fracture, infection, paralysis (rare), hardware failure, battery depletion, CSF leak. Cost: high (~£10-20k) but cost-effective if successful.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
MechanismEpidural electrical stimulation of dorsal columns; modulates pain signals
IndicationsFBSS (most common), CRPS, refractory neuropathic pain, refractory angina
Patient selectionTrial (temporary electrode, 1-2 weeks) → permanent if ≥50% relief
ModesTraditional (paresthesia), high-frequency 10kHz (paresthesia-free), burst, DRG
EvidenceGood for FBSS, CRPS; less for other
MultidisciplinaryPain, neurosurgery, psychology
ContraindicationsUntreated psychiatric, drug abuse, infection, anticoagulation
ComplicationsLead migration, fracture, infection, paralysis (rare), hardware failure
BatteryRechargeable (10 years), non-rechargeable (replace)

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
PNSPeripheral nerve stimulation, localised pain
IT pumpFor nociceptive, opioids, baclofen
Systemic opioidFirst-line, oral/SC/IV

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Mechanism? A: Epidural electrical stimulation of dorsal columns; gate control theory.
  1. 2. Q: Main indications? A: FBSS, CRPS, refractory neuropathic pain.
  1. 3. Q: Trial threshold? A: ≥50% pain reduction.
  1. 4. Q: High-frequency 10kHz? A: Paresthesia-free mode; advantages: no paresthesia, may be preferred.
  1. 5. Q: Complications? A: Lead migration, infection, paralysis (rare), hardware failure, battery depletion.

▸ 9.4.07 Trigeminal Neuralgia Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Neuropathic Pain

1. 1. Definition

Trigeminal neuralgia (tic douloureux) is a chronic neuropathic pain disorder characterised by sudden, severe, brief, electric shock-like or stabbing unilateral facial pain in the distribution of one or more branches of the trigeminal nerve (V2, V3 most common). Triggered by light touch, chewing, talking, brushing teeth, cold air. Cause: most often idiopathic or due to neurovascular compression (superior cerebellar artery); secondary: tumour, MS, post-herpetic. Treatment: carbamazepine (first-line); oxcarbazepine, baclofen, gabapentin, lamotrigine; surgery (microvascular decompression, glycerol rhizotomy, balloon compression, radiofrequency).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Trigeminal neuralgia (tic douloureux) is a chronic neuropathic pain disorder of the trigeminal nerve (CN V) with sudden, severe, brief (seconds-2 min), electric shock-like or stabbing unilateral facial pain in V2/V3 distribution (V1 rare). Triggers: light touch, chewing, talking, brushing teeth, cold air, shaving. May be primary (idiopathic, neurovascular compression of root entry zone by superior cerebellar artery) or secondary (tumour — cerebellopontine angle, MS — bilateral, post-herpetic, trauma).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Clinical diagnosis (typical features). MRI brain with trigeminal nerve protocol to exclude secondary cause (tumour, MS, vascular malformation). LP if MS suspected.

2. Management

First-line: carbamazepine 100-200mg BD titrated to 400-1200mg/day (effective in 70% initially, may lose efficacy with time); oxcarbazepine 300-600mg BD (similar efficacy, better tolerated). Second-line: baclofen 5-10mg TDS, lamotrigine 100-400mg/day (slow titration), gabapentin 300-3600mg/day. Refractory: microvascular decompression (Jannetta procedure, 80% long-term success, 1% mortality/0.5% stroke); percutaneous procedures (glycerol rhizotomy, balloon compression, radiofrequency thermocoagulation); stereotactic radiosurgery (Gamma knife).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
TypeTrigeminal nerve (V) neuropathic pain
SiteV2, V3 (V1 rare)
CharacterElectric, stabbing, paroxysmal
TriggerTouch, chewing, talking, brushing teeth
CauseNeurovascular compression (SCA); MS; tumour
First-lineCarbamazepine (or oxcarbazepine)
Second-lineBaclofen, gabapentin, lamotrigine
SurgeryMicrovascular decompression, glycerol, balloon, RFA
MSYounger, bilateral

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Cluster headacheLonger, autonomic features
TMDContinuous, jaw
Dental painLocalised, triggers on bite
PHNV1, post-herpetic
TumourPersistent, neurological signs

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: First-line treatment for trigeminal neuralgia? A: Carbamazepine (or oxcarbazepine).
  1. 2. Q: Most common cause? A: Neurovascular compression (superior cerebellar artery).
  1. 3. Q: When to suspect MS as cause? A: Bilateral or young patient (under 50).
  1. 4. Q: Most common divisions involved? A: V2 (maxillary) and V3 (mandibular); V1 (ophthalmic) rare.
  1. 5. Q: Surgical options? A: Microvascular decompression (Jannetta) — first-line surgery; percutaneous procedures (glycerol, balloon, radiofrequency); Gamma knife.

▸ 9.5 Cancer Pain Syndromes 10
▸ 9.5.01 Bone Pain In Cancer Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Cancer Pain Syndromes

1. 1. Definition

Bone pain is the most common cancer pain syndrome, caused by primary bone tumours or, more commonly, bone metastases (breast, prostate, lung, kidney, thyroid, multiple myeloma). Pathophysiology: osteoclast activation, inflammatory mediators, periosteal stretching, microfractures, nerve entrapment. Features: deep, aching, worse on movement/weight-bearing (incident pain), constant background. Treatment: WHO ladder + NSAIDs, bisphosphonates (zoledronic acid 4mg IV q3-4w) or denosumab (120mg SC q4w), radiotherapy (local, hemibody), radiopharmaceuticals (strontium-89, samarium-153, radium-223 for prostate), orthopaedic stabilisation.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Bone pain in cancer is common (60-80% of metastatic cancer, especially breast, prostate, lung, kidney, thyroid, myeloma). Mechanisms: tumour-induced osteolysis, osteoblast activation, mechanical instability, periosteal stretching, pathological fracture, nerve compression, cytokine release. Pain: deep, aching, worse on weight-bearing/movement, often worse at night. May be incident pain (movement-related). Pathological fracture is a major complication.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Imaging: X-ray (lytic/blastic lesion, but needs 30-50% bone loss to be visible), CT (early bone destruction), MRI (soft tissue + marrow, most sensitive), bone scan (Tc-99m, sensitive for metastases). Bloods: calcium (hypercalcaemia), alkaline phosphatase (bone turnover), FBC (anaemia, marrow infiltration), PSA (prostate), paraprotein (myeloma). Biopsy for histology if primary unknown.

2. Management

Multimodal: WHO ladder + adjuvant analgesia; bone-targeted agents (bisphosphonates — zoledronic acid 4mg IV monthly, denosumab 120mg SC monthly, both reduce SREs and pain); radiotherapy (external beam for localised pain, 8Gy single fraction or 20Gy/5 fractions; radiopharmaceuticals — strontium-89, samarium-153 for widespread bone mets); surgery for instability/pathological fracture; cementoplasty (vertebroplasty, kyphoplasty for vertebral lesions); interventional (nerve blocks, intrathecal); supportive (mobility, weight-bearing, orthotics).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
CauseMetastases (breast, prostate, lung, kidney, thyroid, myeloma)
PathophysiologyOsteoclast, inflammation, periosteal stretch, microfracture
FeatureDeep, aching, incident pain
TreatmentWHO ladder + NSAID
BisphosphonatesZoledronate 4mg IV q3-4w
Denosumab120mg SC q4w
RadiotherapyLocal, hemibody
RadiopharmaceuticalsSr-89, Sm-153, Ra-223

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Primary bone tumourBiopsy
Osteoporotic fractureDEXA, history
Bone infection (TB, etc.)Biopsy, bloods

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Most common cancers with bone metastases? A: Breast, prostate, lung, kidney, thyroid, myeloma.
  1. 2. Q: Bone-targeted therapy options? A: Bisphosphonates (zoledronic acid), denosumab (anti-RANKL); reduce SREs and pain.
  1. 3. Q: Radiotherapy doses for bone pain? A: Single 8Gy (most common) or 20Gy/5 fractions; radiopharmaceuticals (strontium-89, samarium-153) for widespread.
  1. 4. Q: Mechanism of bone pain? A: Tumour osteolysis, osteoblast activation, periosteal stretching, mechanical instability, cytokine release.
  1. 5. Q: Vertebral augmentation? A: Vertebroplasty/kyphoplasty for vertebral compression fractures; cement injection; rapid pain relief.

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.5.02 Breakthrough Pain And End Of Dose Failure Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Cancer Pain Syndromes

1. 1. Definition

Breakthrough pain (BTP) is a transient exacerbation of pain on a background of adequately controlled baseline pain. Subtypes: spontaneous (idiopathic, no trigger), incident (triggered by activity), end-of-dose failure (regular analgesic wears off before next dose). Common in cancer pain. Treatment: identify and treat (end-of-dose failure = increase background opioid or shorten interval; incident = rescue before activity; spontaneous = rescue as needed). Rescue dose: 10% of total daily opioid dose, q1h PRN. Rapid-onset fentanyl formulations for incident and spontaneous BTP.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Breakthrough cancer pain (BTcP) is a transient exacerbation of pain that occurs on a background of otherwise stable, controlled pain. Features: rapid onset (minutes), severe intensity, short duration (median 30 min), self-limiting, often unpredictable, often related to activity (incident) or spontaneous. End-of-dose failure: pain at end of dosing interval of regular opioid, indicating inadequate dose or interval. Both reduce QoL, function, mood.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Characterise pain: background, breakthrough (number, severity, onset, duration, triggers, predictability, relief by rescue dose). Distinguish breakthrough from end-of-dose failure, uncontrolled background pain, or new pain cause. Use breakthrough pain assessment tools.

2. Management

Breakthrough: rescue dose = 1/6 (10-15%) of total daily opioid, fast-acting. Use IR opioid (oral morphine 30 min onset, or transmucosal fentanyl for rapid onset 5-15 min — for opioid-tolerant patients with ≥60mg morphine/day). Treat incident pain: pre-empt with rescue dose before activity. End-of-dose failure: increase regular dose or shorten interval. Optimise background pain control. Avoid frequent breakthrough (>3-4/day) — indicates poor background control.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
DefinitionTransient exacerbation on controlled baseline
SubtypesSpontaneous, incident, end-of-dose failure
End-of-doseWear-off before next dose
TreatmentEnd-of-dose: ↑ background or ↓ interval
Rescue10% of total daily dose, q1h PRN
Rapid-onsetSublingual/buccal/intranasal fentanyl
Common inCancer pain

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Uncontrolled background painConstant, not transient
New pain causeNew site/character
End-of-dose failurePredictable, before next regular dose

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Breakthrough pain rescue dose? A: 1/6 (10-15%) of total daily opioid.
  1. 2. Q: Transmucosal fentanyl indication? A: Opioid-tolerant (≥60mg oral morphine/day) breakthrough cancer pain; rapid onset 5-15 min.
  1. 3. Q: Distinction breakthrough vs end-of-dose failure? A: Breakthrough = transient, often unpredictable; end-of-dose = predictable at end of dosing interval (need shorter interval or higher dose).
  1. 4. Q: If >3-4 breakthrough doses/day? A: Indicates poor background pain control; increase regular dose.
  1. 5. Q: Incident pain management? A: Pre-empt with rescue dose 15-30 min before activity.

▸ 9.5.04 Cancer Pain Syndromes Summary Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Cancer Pain Syndromes

1. 1. Definition

Cancer pain syndromes can be categorised by mechanism: nociceptive (somatic, visceral), neuropathic (plexus infiltration, MSCC, PHN, chemotherapy-induced), mixed (most common), incident (movement-triggered), breakthrough (spontaneous, end-of-dose). Common cancer pain syndromes: bone metastases (incident pain, NSAID+bisphosphonates+RT), pancreatic cancer (visceral, coeliac plexus), MSCC (emergency, dexamethasone), brachial/lumbosacral plexopathy (neuropathic), malignant bowel obstruction, post-radiation (radiation-induced, MISP), chemotherapy-induced neuropathy, malignant wound. Stepwise approach: WHO ladder + adjuvant, opioid rotation, breakthrough dose, interventional (nerve blocks, IT analgesia), palliative RT, palliative sedation.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Cancer pain syndromes are classified by mechanism and site: nociceptive (somatic — bone, soft tissue; visceral — organ capsular, hollow viscus), neuropathic (nerve compression, infiltration, post-treatment), incident (movement-related, e.g., pathological fracture), breakthrough (transient exacerbation on stable background). Common syndromes: bone metastases (60-80%), neuropathic (30-40% — plexus infiltration, post-radiation, post-chemo), visceral (pancreatic, hepatic, peritoneal), incident (pathological fracture, movement).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Comprehensive pain assessment: site, character, mechanism (nociceptive vs neuropathic), triggers, temporal pattern, associated features. Disease-specific imaging, biomarkers. Multidisciplinary team assessment.

2. Management

Total pain concept: physical + psychological + social + spiritual. WHO ladder + adjuvants (gabapentin for neuropathic, bisphosphonates for bone, anticholinergics for colic). Disease-modifying: surgery, chemotherapy, radiotherapy. Interventional: nerve blocks, intrathecal pump, spinal cord stimulation. Non-pharm: TENS, CBT, physiotherapy, complementary therapy. Psychosocial and spiritual support.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
CategoriesNociceptive, neuropathic, mixed, incident, breakthrough
BoneMost common; incident; NSAID+bisphosphonate+RT
PancreasVisceral; coeliac plexus block
MSCCEmergency; dexamethasone; surgery/RT
PlexopathyBrachial, lumbosacral
MBOBowel obstruction; conservative management
Chemo-induced PNTaxanes, platinums, vinca
MISPPost-radiation; painful myelopathy
ApproachWHO ladder + adjuvant; rotation; rescue; interventional; RT; sedation

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Acute painPost-operative, procedure, fracture
Chronic non-cancer painDifferent mechanism

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Most common cancer pain syndrome? A: Bone metastases (60-80%); neuropathic 30-40%.
  1. 2. Q: 4 main mechanisms of cancer pain? A: Nociceptive (somatic, visceral), neuropathic, mixed, incident.
  1. 3. Q: Most common cancers with bone mets? A: Breast, prostate, lung, kidney, thyroid, myeloma.
  1. 4. Q: Cancer-related neuropathic pain causes? A: Tumour compression/infiltration, surgery, chemotherapy (vinca, taxane, platinum), radiotherapy.
  1. 5. Q: WHO ladder? A: Mild → paracetamol ± NSAID; Moderate → weak opioid (codeine, tramadol); Severe → strong opioid (morphine, oxycodone, fentanyl).

▸ 9.5.05 Coeliac Plexus Block Cpb Full

Related: [[../../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

[!info] Davidson alignment: Chapter 8: Pain and Palliative Care (24th Edition, Colvin, Fallon) — Cancer Pain Syndromes

1. 1. Definition

Coeliac plexus block (CPB) is an interventional technique for visceral pain from upper abdominal organs (pancreas, liver, gallbladder, spleen, proximal small bowel). The coeliac plexus is a network of sympathetic nerves in the retroperitoneum at the level of the celiac trunk. Block: needle inserted posteriorly (posterior approach) under CT/ultrasound guidance, or via EUS guidance; injectate: local anaesthetic + corticosteroid (block, diagnostic and short-term) or absolute alcohol/phenol (neurolysis, longer-lasting). Indications: pancreatic cancer pain (most common, 70-90% effective for visceral component), chronic pancreatitis, intra-abdominal malignancy. Side effects: hypotension (sympathetic block), diarrhoea (loss of sympathetic tone), back pain, paraplegia (rare, anterior spinal artery injury), pneumothorax, infection. Effective, well-tolerated, opioid-sparing.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Coeliac plexus block (CPB) is an interventional technique for visceral pain from upper abdominal organs (pancreas, liver, gallbladder, spleen, proximal small bowel). The coeliac plexus is a sympathetic network in the retroperitoneum at the level of the celiac trunk (T12-L1). Block: needle posteriorly (CT/ultrasound-guided, posterior approach) or via endoscopic ultrasound (EUS, anterior approach) to celiac plexus; injectate: local anaesthetic + corticosteroid (block, diagnostic, short-term) or absolute alcohol/phenol (neurolysis, longer-lasting). Indications: pancreatic cancer pain (most common, 70-90% effective for visceral component), chronic pancreatitis, intra-abdominal malignancy. Side effects: hypotension (sympathetic block, common), diarrhoea (loss of sympathetic tone), back pain, paraplegia (rare, anterior spinal artery injury), pneumothorax, infection.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Indication: pancreatic or upper abdominal cancer pain, refractory to systemic opioid. Imaging: CT, ultrasound, or EUS for guidance. Coagulation: PT, aPTT, platelets (stop anticoagulation). Renal function: avoid dehydration; pre-hydrate. Pain assessment, bowel function, BP monitoring.

2. Management

Procedure: CT/USS-guided posterior or EUS-guided anterior approach; needle to celiac plexus; inject LA + corticosteroid (block) or alcohol/phenol (neurolysis). Pre: IV hydration, monitoring, consent. During: BP, pain, comfort. Post: monitor BP, pain, bowel function, neurological signs. Side effects: hypotension (IV fluids, vasopressor), diarrhoea (loperamide, antispasmodic), back pain. Complications: paraplegia (urgent decompression, MRI), pneumothorax, infection. Effective for visceral pain 70-90%; less for somatic (consider other blocks).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
TargetCoeliac plexus (sympathetic network at celiac trunk)
IndicationsPancreatic cancer pain (70-90% effective), chronic pancreatitis, upper abdominal visceral pain
ApproachesPosterior (CT/USS-guided), anterior (EUS-guided, transabdominal), trans-aortic
InjectateBlock: LA + corticosteroid (short-term); neurolysis: alcohol/phenol (longer-lasting)
Efficacy70-90% effective for pancreatic cancer pain
Side effectsHypotension, diarrhoea, back pain, paraplegia (rare), pneumothorax, infection
Paraplegia riskAnterior spinal artery injury (rare); careful technique
HydrationIV fluids pre/post to prevent hypotension
MonitoringBP, pain, bowel function, neurological signs
Versus opioidsOpioid-sparing, better QoL, less side effects

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Other nerve blocksDifferent sites for different pains
IT pumpWider coverage, more complex
Systemic opioidFirst-line before CPB

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Most common indication? A: Pancreatic cancer pain (70-90% effective).
  1. 2. Q: Block vs neurolysis? A: Block = LA + corticosteroid (short-term, diagnostic); neurolysis = alcohol/phenol (longer-lasting).
  1. 3. Q: Approaches? A: Posterior (CT/USS-guided), anterior (EUS-guided), trans-aortic.
  1. 4. Q: Common side effects? A: Hypotension, diarrhoea, back pain.
  1. 5. Q: Serious complication? A: Paraplegia (anterior spinal artery injury, rare); MRI if neurological symptoms.

▸ 9.5.06 Incident Pain Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Cancer Pain Syndromes

1. 1. Definition

Incident pain is a subtype of breakthrough pain that is triggered by a specific event or activity (movement, coughing, weight-bearing, dressing changes, swallowing). Common in bone metastases (incident on weight-bearing), post-surgical, post-radiation, mucositis, neuropathic pain. Often severe, transient (peaks 3-10 min, lasts ~30 min), with rapid onset. Treatment: optimal background opioid; rescue dose (10% of total daily dose); rapid-onset opioids (sublingual fentanyl — Abstral, Effentora, PecFent, intranasal fentanyl); activity modification; radiotherapy for bone metastases; preventive analgesia (NSAID 30-60 min before activity).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Incident pain (volitional or non-volitional) is pain triggered by an activity or event (movement, cough, dressing, weight-bearing, swallowing, defecation) in a patient with otherwise stable background pain. Common in bone metastases (movement), neuropathic (e.g., dressing-related in PHN), and procedural pain. Predictable (volitional) or unpredictable (non-volitional).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Characterise trigger and predictability. Distinguish from breakthrough, end-of-dose, and background pain.

2. Management

Pre-empt with rescue opioid dose 15-30 min before activity (volitional incident). For non-volitional, consider immediate-onset opioid (sublingual, buccal, intranasal fentanyl). Optimise background pain control. Non-pharm: pacing, positioning, physiotherapy, immobilisation, supportive devices. Interventional: nerve blocks for predictable incident pain (e.g., coeliac plexus for pancreatic cancer).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
TypeTriggered (incident) breakthrough pain
CauseMovement, cough, weight-bearing, dressing
Common inBone metastases, mucositis, post-op
OnsetRapid (3-10 min), short (~30 min)
TreatmentBackground opioid + rescue
Rescue10% of total daily dose
Rapid-onset fentanylAbstral, Effentora, PecFent, Instanyl
RTFor bone metastases
PreventionNSAID 30-60 min pre-activity

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Breakthrough painSpontaneous, less predictable
End-of-dose failurePredictable, before next regular dose
Uncontrolled backgroundConstant, not activity-related

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Definition of incident pain? A: Pain triggered by activity or event in patient with stable background pain.
  1. 2. Q: Management of volitional incident pain? A: Pre-empt with rescue dose 15-30 min before activity.
  1. 3. Q: Immediate-onset opioid for non-volitional? A: Sublingual, buccal, or intranasal fentanyl (rapid onset 5-15 min).
  1. 4. Q: Common cause in cancer? A: Bone metastases, pathological fracture, neuropathic (plexus, nerve).
  1. 5. Q: Interventional option for predictable incident pain? A: Nerve blocks (e.g., coeliac plexus block for pancreatic cancer — pre-empts pain).

▸ 9.5.07 Intrathecal Analgesia Intrathecal Pump Neuraxial Full

Related: [[../../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

[!info] Davidson alignment: Chapter 8: Pain and Palliative Care (24th Edition, Colvin, Fallon) — Cancer Pain Syndromes

1. 1. Definition

Intrathecal (IT) analgesia is delivery of analgesics directly into the cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) via implanted pump, for refractory cancer pain (2-4% of cancer patients), severe spasticity, chronic non-malignant pain. Drugs: morphine, hydromorphone, fentanyl, sufentanil, bupivacaine, clonidine, baclofen (spasticity), ziconotide (non-opioid, severe pain). Indications: cancer pain refractory to systemic opioid (dose-limiting side effects, inadequate analgesia). Advantages: targeted, lower doses (e.g., IT morphine 1/300 of oral dose), fewer systemic side effects, can combine drugs. Complications: spinal haematoma (rare, urgent decompression), infection (meningitis, abscess), CSF leak, catheter migration/breakage, granuloma (high-concentration opioid), overdose, withdrawal (pump failure). Requires specialist team (anaesthesia/pain, neurosurgery, palliative).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Intrathecal (IT) analgesia is delivery of analgesics directly into the cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) via implanted pump and catheter, for refractory cancer pain (2-4% of cancer patients), severe spasticity, chronic non-malignant pain. Drugs: morphine, hydromorphone, fentanyl, sufentanil, bupivacaine, clonidine, baclofen (spasticity), ziconotide (non-opioid, severe pain, N-type calcium channel blocker). Indications: cancer pain refractory to systemic opioid (dose-limiting side effects, inadequate analgesia). Advantages: targeted, lower doses (e.g., IT morphine 1/300 of oral dose), fewer systemic side effects, can combine drugs (opioid + local anaesthetic + clonidine).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Trial: temporary IT catheter for 1-2 weeks; if >50% pain relief, proceed to permanent implant. Imaging: MRI spine (rule out lesion, assess epidural/intrathecal space). Coagulation: stop anticoagulation. Infection screen. Pain assessment, function, side effects, drug history, opioid dose.

2. Management

Trial: temporary IT catheter, 1-2 weeks; >50% pain reduction → proceed. Permanent implant: pump (subcutaneous abdominal wall), catheter (intrathecal, often at L1-L2, tip at appropriate level for pain — cervical, thoracic, lumbar based on pain location). Drug selection: morphine (most common, hydrophilic, long CSF residence), hydromorphone (alternative), fentanyl/sufentanil (lipophilic, less rostral spread), baclofen (spasticity), ziconotide (refractory). Combinations: opioid + bupivacaine, opioid + clonidine. Dose titration: start low, titrate slowly. Refill: every 1-3 months (intervals decrease at low concentrations).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
IndicationRefractory cancer pain (2-4% of cancer patients), severe spasticity, chronic non-malignant pain
DrugsMorphine, hydromorphone, fentanyl, sufentanil, bupivacaine, clonidine, baclofen, ziconotide
DoseIT morphine 1/300 of oral dose (e.g., 1mg IT = 300mg PO)
AdvantagesTargeted, lower doses, fewer side effects, can combine
ComplicationsSpinal haematoma (urgent decompression), infection (meningitis, abscess), CSF leak, catheter migration, granuloma, overdose, withdrawal
GranulomaHigh-concentration opioid, usually at catheter tip, may cause progressive weakness, MRI
Pump failureWithdrawal (severe); missed refills, battery depletion, catheter issues
TrialTemporary IT catheter for 1-2 weeks; if >50% relief, proceed to permanent
SpecialistAnaesthesia/pain, neurosurgery, palliative
MonitoringPain, function, side effects, refill, battery

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
EpiduralLess potent, larger volume
SCSElectrical stimulation, neuropathic
CSCIContinuous SC, simpler

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Indications? A: Refractory cancer pain (2-4%), severe spasticity, chronic non-malignant pain.
  1. 2. Q: IT morphine vs PO morphine dose? A: IT 1/300 of PO dose (1mg IT = 300mg PO).
  1. 3. Q: Complications? A: Spinal haematoma, infection, CSF leak, catheter migration, granuloma, overdose, withdrawal.
  1. 4. Q: What is granuloma? A: Mass at catheter tip from high-concentration opioid, may cause progressive weakness, MRI.
  1. 5. Q: Why use IT? A: Targeted, lower doses, fewer systemic side effects, can combine drugs.

▸ 9.5.08 Neuropathic Cancer Pain Plexus Infiltration Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Cancer Pain Syndromes

1. 1. Definition

Neuropathic cancer pain in cancer arises from direct nerve compression, infiltration, or damage by tumour, often involving brachial plexus (Pancoast, breast), lumbosacral plexus (pelvic cancers — cervical, prostate, colorectal), or peripheral nerves. Features: burning, electric, shooting, allodynia in nerve distribution. Often combined with somatic pain. Treatment: WHO ladder + adjuvant (TCAs, gabapentin, pregabalin — first-line for neuropathic); corticosteroids (dexamethasone) for compression; radiotherapy for tumour shrinkage; chemotherapy; nerve blocks; intrathecal analgesia.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Neuropathic cancer pain from nerve infiltration/compression is common (plexus — brachial or lumbosacral; spinal cord compression; peripheral nerves). Brachial plexus infiltration: lung apex (Pancoast), breast, lymphoma. Lumbosacral plexus: colorectal, gynaecological, retroperitoneal tumours. Features: burning, shooting, electric, lancinating in nerve distribution, with sensory changes, motor weakness, autonomic changes, allodynia. Often severe, with neuropathic components poorly responsive to opioids alone.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

MRI (brachial/lumbosacral plexus, spine) to identify cause; nerve conduction studies; PET-CT to assess disease activity. Pain assessment, function.

2. Management

WHO ladder + adjuvants (gabapentin, pregabalin, amitriptyline, duloxetine). Opioid rotation if inadequate. Radiotherapy for radiosensitive tumours (lymphoma, breast, small cell lung) — often gives rapid relief. Chemotherapy if appropriate. Interventional: nerve blocks (brachial plexus block), intrathecal pump, spinal cord stimulation. Surgery for decompression if acute compression (e.g., spinal cord).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
CauseTumour infiltration/compression of nerve
SitesBrachial (Pancoast, breast), lumbosacral (pelvic)
FeatureBurning, electric, allodynia in distribution
TreatmentWHO + adjuvant (TCA, gabapentinoid)
CorticosteroidDexamethasone for compression
RT/CTTumour shrinkage
Nerve blockFor refractory pain

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Radiation plexopathyLate complication, painless, progressive
Post-radiation neuropathyAfter RT, often months-years
MSCCCord compression, sensory level, motor

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Most common cause of brachial plexopathy in cancer? A: Pancoast tumour (lung apex), breast cancer, lymphoma.
  1. 2. Q: First-line adjuvant for neuropathic cancer pain? A: Gabapentin, pregabalin, amitriptyline, or duloxetine.
  1. 3. Q: Interventional option? A: Brachial plexus block, intrathecal pump, spinal cord stimulation.
  1. 4. Q: When is radiotherapy useful? A: Radiosensitive tumours (lymphoma, breast, small cell lung); often rapid relief.
  1. 5. Q: Lumbosacral plexopathy causes? A: Colorectal, gynaecological (cervix, ovary), retroperitoneal, lymphomas.

▸ 9.5.09 Patient Controlled Analgesia Pca Full

Related: [[../../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

[!info] Davidson alignment: Chapter 8: Pain and Palliative Care (24th Edition, Colvin, Fallon) — Cancer Pain Syndromes

1. 1. Definition

Patient-controlled analgesia (PCA) is a method of pain control allowing the patient to self-administer boluses of analgesic (typically IV or SC) within preset limits, using a programmable pump. Used for severe acute postoperative pain, trauma, burn, acute crisis in palliative care, labour. Drugs: morphine, fentanyl, hydromorphone, oxycodone. Settings: bolus dose (e.g., morphine 1-2mg), lockout interval (5-10 min), 4-hour limit (optional, safety), background infusion (controversial for opioid-naive; useful for chronic pain/cancer). Advantages: patient autonomy, on-demand analgesia, reduced anxiety, less time to peak effect, lower total opioid use. Safe with appropriate monitoring (respiratory rate, sedation, oxygen). Contraindications: confused, unable to use pump, severe respiratory disease (cautious). PCA is not for end-of-life (use CSCI).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Patient-controlled analgesia (PCA) is a method of pain control allowing the patient to self-administer boluses of analgesic (typically IV or SC) within preset limits, using a programmable pump. Used for severe acute postoperative pain, trauma, burn, acute crisis in palliative care, labour. Drugs: morphine, fentanyl, hydromorphone, oxycodone. Settings: bolus dose (e.g., morphine 1-2mg IV), lockout interval (5-10 min), 4-hour limit (optional, safety), background infusion (controversial for opioid-naive; useful for chronic pain/cancer). Advantages: patient autonomy, on-demand analgesia, reduced anxiety, less time to peak effect, lower total opioid use. Safe with appropriate monitoring.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Pain assessment, opioid tolerance (if cancer pain), sedation (Pasero scale), respiratory rate (every 1-2h initially), oxygen saturation, blood pressure. Patient education (how to use pump). Lockout, bolus, 4-hour limit, background infusion parameters. Total opioid use (24h), effectiveness. Complications: respiratory depression, sedation, opioid side effects, equipment failure, programming error.

2. Management

Indication: severe acute pain (post-operative, trauma, labour, cancer crisis) with conscious patient able to use pump. Settings: bolus dose (morphine 1-2mg IV, fentanyl 25-50mcg IV), lockout interval (5-10 min), 4-hour limit (optional, safety), background infusion (controversial for opioid-naive; useful for chronic pain/cancer — e.g., morphine 1-2mg/h). Monitoring: RR, sedation, pain, total use. Side effect management: laxative, antiemetic, naloxone for RD (3-5mcg IV, titrate). PCA is NOT for end-of-life (use CSCI). Contraindications: confused, unable to use pump, severe respiratory disease (cautious).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
MechanismSelf-administered bolus within preset limits, programmable pump
RoutesIV (most common), SC (palliative), epidural, peripheral nerve catheter
DrugsMorphine, fentanyl, hydromorphone, oxycodone
SettingsBolus dose, lockout interval, 4-hour limit, background infusion (optional)
Boluse.g., morphine 1-2mg IV
Lockout5-10 min
BackgroundControversial for opioid-naive; useful for chronic pain/cancer
AdvantagesPatient autonomy, on-demand, reduced anxiety, lower total opioid
Safe ifAppropriate monitoring (RR, sedation, O2)
Vs CSCIPCA for acute/severe with conscious patient; CSCI for dying, oral not possible
End of lifeNot for end-of-life (use CSCI)

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
CSCIContinuous, for dying, oral not possible
IT pumpRefractory, invasive
Intermittent IM/SC opioidOlder, less effective

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: What is PCA? A: Patient-controlled bolus of analgesic within preset limits, programmable pump.
  1. 2. Q: Common settings? A: Bolus (morphine 1-2mg IV), lockout 5-10 min, 4-hour limit (optional), background (controversial).
  1. 3. Q: Advantages? A: Patient autonomy, on-demand, reduced anxiety, lower total opioid.
  1. 4. Q: Vs CSCI? A: PCA for conscious patient, severe acute, post-op; CSCI for dying, oral not possible.
  1. 5. Q: Contraindications? A: Confused, unable to use pump, severe respiratory disease (cautious).

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.5.10 Visceral Cancer Pain Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Cancer Pain Syndromes

1. 1. Definition

Visceral cancer pain arises from infiltration, distension, compression, or ischaemia of viscera (liver, pancreas, bowel, bladder, kidney). Features: deep, dull, diffuse, poorly localised; may be referred (e.g., diaphragmatic irritation → shoulder pain, Kehr sign); often associated with autonomic features (N&V, sweating). Specific syndromes: liver capsule pain (right upper quadrant, dull), pancreatic cancer pain (epigastric, back), bowel obstruction (colicky). Treatment: WHO ladder + adjuvant (TCAs for neuropathic components, corticosteroids for capsule stretching, antiemetics), nerve blocks (coeliac plexus for pancreatic), surgery (bypass, stoma).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Visceral cancer pain arises from infiltration, distension, or obstruction of organs (liver, pancreas, bowel, lung, bladder). Common cancers: pancreatic, liver metastases, colorectal, ovarian, lung. Features: diffuse, deep, cramping, squeezing, with autonomic features (sweating, N&V, pallor), often referred to dermatomes (Head's zones), often with muscular guarding. Visceral hyperalgesia in referred zone. Often refractory to opioids alone.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Imaging: CT, MRI, ultrasound. Endoscopy if luminal (OGD, colonoscopy). Biomarkers (CA19-9, CEA, AFP). PET-CT for staging. Laparoscopy if needed.

2. Management

WHO ladder + adjuvants: corticosteroid (liver capsule distension, pancreatic oedema); anticholinergics (hyoscine, hyoscine butylbromide, glycopyrronium for hollow viscus colic, bowel obstruction); antidepressants/neuropathic agents (if neuropathic component). Disease-modifying: chemotherapy, radiotherapy, surgery. Interventional: coeliac plexus block (pancreatic cancer, 70-90% relief), superior hypogastric block (pelvic visceral), neuraxial analgesia (intrathecal/epidural), neurolytic procedures.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
TypeVisceral (organ) pain
FeatureDeep, dull, diffuse, referred
AutonomicN&V, sweating, BP changes
Liver capsuleRUQ, dull, corticosteroids
PancreaticEpigastric, back, coeliac plexus block
Bowel obstructionColicky, N&V, malignant
TreatmentWHO ladder + adjuvant
Coeliac plexusPancreatic cancer pain (procedural)

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Somatic painWell-localised, sharp
NeuropathicBurning, nerve distribution

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Coeliac plexus block for which cancer? A: Pancreatic cancer pain; 70-90% effective.
  1. 2. Q: Adjuvants for visceral pain? A: Corticosteroid (capsule distension), anticholinergics (hollow viscus colic, bowel obstruction).
  1. 3. Q: Referred pain for diaphragmatic irritation? A: Shoulder tip (phrenic nerve C3, C4, C5).
  1. 4. Q: Why visceral pain often refractory to opioids? A: Visceral hyperalgesia, central sensitisation, mixed mechanism.
  1. 5. Q: Superior hypogastric block indication? A: Pelvic visceral pain (cervical, prostate, colorectal).

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.6.02 Bisphosphonates And Denosumab For Bone Pain Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Adjuvant Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Bisphosphonates (e.g., zoledronic acid 4mg IV q3-4w) and denosumab (120mg SC q4w) are antiresorptive agents used to prevent skeletal-related events (SRE) in bone metastases and multiple myeloma, and to reduce bone pain. Mechanism: bisphosphonates inhibit osteoclast activity (bind to bone, taken up by osteoclasts); denosumab is anti-RANKL monoclonal antibody preventing osteoclast formation/activation. Efficacy: delay SRE (pathological fracture, spinal cord compression, hypercalcaemia, radiation/surgery), reduce bone pain. Side effects: hypocalcaemia (correct vitamin D, Ca), renal toxicity (zoledronic acid), osteonecrosis of jaw (ONJ — good dental hygiene), atypical femoral fractures (denosumab), flu-like symptoms (first dose).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Bisphosphonates (zoledronic acid 4mg IV monthly, pamidronate 90mg IV monthly, ibandronate 6mg IV monthly) and denosumab (120mg SC monthly) inhibit osteoclast-mediated bone resorption, reducing skeletal-related events (SREs — fracture, hypercalcaemia, spinal cord compression, need for RT/surgery) and pain in bone metastases (breast, prostate, lung, kidney, thyroid, myeloma). Onset of pain relief: 1-2 weeks; full effect 4-12 weeks. Side effects: flu-like symptoms (acute phase reaction with first dose, especially zoledronic acid), hypocalcaemia, renal toxicity (zoledronic acid — check eGFR, monitor), osteonecrosis of the jaw (ONJ — 1-2%, risk with dental procedures, poor oral hygiene), atypical femoral fractures (long-term).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Baseline: dental review (mandatory before), calcium, phosphate, vitamin D, renal function, FBC, bone scan. Monitor: calcium, renal function, dental symptoms. Stop if severe hypocalcaemia, ONJ, atypical fracture.

2. Management

Denosumab preferred in renal impairment (no nephrotoxicity). Zoledronic acid contraindicated if eGFR<30. Monitor renal function. Dental hygiene. Calcium and vitamin D supplementation. Treat hypocalcaemia (calcium IV/oral, vitamin D). Onset of action 1-2 weeks.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
BisphosphonateZoledronic acid 4mg IV q3-4w
Denosumab120mg SC q4w
MechanismAnti-osteoclast (bisphosphonate); anti-RANKL (denosumab)
IndicationBone metastases, myeloma, hypercalcaemia
EfficacyDelay SRE, reduce pain
Side effectsHypocalcaemia, renal, ONJ, atypical fracture, flu-like
PrecautionsCa, vitamin D; dental review; renal monitoring
DenosumabNot renally cleared

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
RadiotherapyLocalised, often faster
OpioidsDifferent mechanism, more side effects
SurgeryFor instability, fracture

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Difference bisphosphonate vs denosumab? A: Bisphosphonate incorporates into bone, long-lasting; denosumab is monoclonal Ab against RANKL, no renal toxicity, reversibly stops osteoclasts.
  1. 2. Q: ONJ risk? A: 1-2%; risk factors: dental procedures, poor oral hygiene, chemotherapy, steroids; need baseline dental review and ongoing dental care.
  1. 3. Q: Renal dose adjustment? A: Zoledronic acid: avoid eGFR<30; pamidronate: 90→60mg if mild impairment; denosumab: no adjustment (safer in renal failure).
  1. 4. Q: How long for pain relief? A: 1-2 weeks onset, full effect 4-12 weeks.
  1. 5. Q: Hypocalcaemia risk? A: Yes; check baseline calcium, vitamin D; supplement if needed.

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.6.03 Carbamazepine And Oxcarbazepine Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Adjuvant Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Carbamazepine (and oxcarbazepine) are anticonvulsants used as first-line for trigeminal neuralgia. Mechanism: voltage-gated sodium channel blockade. Dose: carbamazepine 100-200mg BD, titrate to 400-800mg BD; oxcarbazepine 300mg BD, titrate to 600-1200mg BD. Side effects: drowsiness, ataxia, diplopia, hyponatraemia (oxcarbazepine, especially elderly), agranulocytosis (rare — FBC monitoring), aplastic anaemia, Stevens-Johnson syndrome (HLA-B*15:02 in Asian populations, FDA black box warning), CYP450 inducer (drug interactions). Contraindicated in: AV block, porphyria, MAOI.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Carbamazepine and oxcarbazepine are voltage-gated sodium channel blockers, first-line for trigeminal neuralgia (carbamazepine effective in 70% initially, oxcarbazepine similar efficacy, better tolerated). Also effective for glossopharyngeal neuralgia, paroxysmal symptoms in MS. Carbamazepine: 100-200mg BD, titrate to 400-1200mg/day. Oxcarbazepine: 300-600mg BD. Side effects: dizziness, ataxia, sedation, diplopia, hyponatraemia (more with oxcarbazepine, especially in elderly), agranulocytosis, aplastic anaemia, severe dermatological reactions (SJS/TEN, especially HLA-B*15:02 in Asian populations — screen before), hepatic, induces CYP450 (carbamazepine, not oxcarbazepine).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

FBC, LFT, U&E (sodium — SIADH), plasma levels (carbamazepine therapeutic 4-12mg/L), HLA-B*15:02 in Asian populations (before carbamazepine), ECG in cardiac history. Monitor for skin reactions.

2. Management

Start low, titrate slowly (1-2 weekly). Take with food. Counsel on signs of bone marrow suppression (fever, sore throat, bruising). Avoid in: AV block, porphyria, severe hepatic disease, concurrent MAOI. Oxcarbazepine advantages: less CYP450 induction, no epoxide metabolite (less interaction with other drugs).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ClassNa channel blocker; anticonvulsant
UseFirst-line trigeminal neuralgia
DoseCarbamazepine 100-200mg BD → 400-800mg BD
Oxcarbazepine300mg BD → 600-1200mg BD
Side effectsSedation, ataxia, hyponatraemia (oxcarb)
FBCAgranulocytosis, aplastic anaemia (rare)
HLA-B*15:02SJS risk in Asian; FDA warning
CYP450Inducer; multiple drug interactions

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
GabapentinDifferent mechanism, less SJS risk
BaclofenGABA-B agonist, can combine

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: First-line for trigeminal neuralgia? A: Carbamazepine (oxcarbazepine similar, better tolerated).
  1. 2. Q: HLA testing for carbamazepine? A: HLA-B*15:02 in Asian populations (Han Chinese, Thai, Indian) due to SJS/TEN risk.
  1. 3. Q: Carbamazepine vs oxcarbazepine? A: Oxcarbazepine better tolerated, less CYP450 induction, no epoxide metabolite; carbamazepine more potent.
  1. 4. Q: Why hyponatraemia? A: SIADH (more with oxcarbazepine); monitor sodium, especially elderly.
  1. 5. Q: Warning signs? A: Fever, sore throat, mouth ulcers (bone marrow suppression); rash (SJS/TEN); jaundice (hepatic).

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.6.04 Corticosteroids In Palliative Care Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Adjuvant Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Corticosteroids (dexamethasone most commonly) are used in palliative care for multiple indications: pain (nerve compression, bone pain, raised ICP), anorexia-cachexia, dyspnoea (lymphangitic carcinomatosis, SVCO), nausea (raised ICP, chemical), prevention of cisplatin-induced N&V, stridor (lymphoma). Dose: 2-16mg/day (low-dose for anorexia, high-dose for raised ICP, MSCC). Side effects: hyperglycaemia, mood changes (euphoria, insomnia), Cushing's, osteoporosis, infection, gastric irritation (PPI cover), proximal myopathy. Avoid in: systemic infection (unless treated), uncontrolled diabetes.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Corticosteroids (dexamethasone, prednisolone) are used in palliative care for multiple indications: pain (bone metastases, raised ICP, neuropathic from tumour compression, lymphoedema), nausea (raised ICP, bowel obstruction), anorexia-cachexia, dyspnoea (lymphangitis, SVC), spinal cord compression (high dose to reduce oedema), cerebral oedema. Dexamethasone preferred (less mineralocorticoid, longer duration). Dose: low (2-4mg/day) for anorexia, moderate (4-8mg) for pain/N&V, high (8-16mg) for MSCC, very high (16-24mg) for cerebral oedema, single dose 10-16mg then 4mg q6h IV for MSCC emergency. Side effects: hyperglycaemia, insomnia, agitation/euphoria, weight gain/appetite, peptic ulcer, infection, adrenal suppression, osteoporosis, myopathy, skin atrophy, mood changes.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Fasting glucose, electrolytes, consider H. pylori. Monitor BP, weight, mood. Avoid long-term use; consider PPI cover.

2. Management

Lowest effective dose, shortest time. Once daily morning dosing. PPI cover if risk of GI. Avoid in: peptic ulcer (unless healed, PPI cover), uncontrolled diabetes (caution), active infection, severe osteoporosis. Taper if >2 weeks use to avoid adrenal suppression. Monitor for side effects.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
DexamethasoneMost common (no mineralocorticoid)
Dose2-16mg/day
IndicationsPain, anorexia, dyspnoea, N&V, stridor, MSCC
Side effectsHyperglycaemia, mood, Cushing, infection, gastric
PPIGastric cover
ContraindicationSystemic infection, uncontrolled DM
TimeTrial 3-5 days; review

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Dexamethasone vs methylprednisoloneDifferent potency and duration

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Why dexamethasone vs prednisolone? A: Less mineralocorticoid effect, longer duration, less fluid retention.
  1. 2. Q: Indications in pall care? A: Pain (bone, neuropathic, raised ICP), N&V, anorexia, MSCC, cerebral oedema, SVC, dyspnoea.
  1. 3. Q: MSCC dose? A: 10-16mg IV bolus then 4mg q6h.
  1. 4. Q: Side effects? A: Hyperglycaemia, insomnia, mood, infection, osteoporosis, adrenal suppression, GI ulcer.
  1. 5. Q: Why taper? A: Adrenal suppression (HPA axis) after >2 weeks.

▸ 9.6.05 Gabapentin And Pregabalin Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Adjuvant Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Gabapentinoids (gabapentin, pregabalin) are α2δ calcium channel ligands and first-line adjuvant analgesics for neuropathic pain. Mechanism: bind α2δ subunit of voltage-gated calcium channels in dorsal horn, reducing neurotransmitter release. Gabapentin: 100-300mg TDS, titrate up to 3600mg/day; renally excreted (dose reduction). Pregabalin: 75mg BD, titrate up to 600mg/day; linear PK, more predictable. Side effects: sedation, dizziness, peripheral oedema, ataxia, weight gain, mood changes. Schedule 3 (pregabalin) / Schedule 5 (gabapentin) controlled drugs. First-line for neuropathic pain (NICE).

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Gabapentin and pregabalin are α2δ subunit ligands of voltage-gated calcium channels, reducing excitatory neurotransmitter release (glutamate, substance P). Effective for neuropathic pain (PHN, diabetic neuropathy, central post-stroke pain, trigeminal neuralgia, fibromyalgia). Gabapentin: 100-300mg nocte, titrate to 300-600mg TDS (max 3600mg/day), renally excreted. Pregabalin: 25-75mg BD, titrate to 150-300mg BD (max 600mg/day). Side effects: sedation, dizziness, ataxia, peripheral oedema, weight gain, cognitive impairment, abuse potential (pregabalin Schedule 5 in UK).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

Renal function (dose reduction in eGFR<30), monitor for sedation, ataxia, especially in elderly. Baseline and follow-up mood (depression).

2. Management

Slow titration (start low, increase every 3-7 days). Take with food. Counsel on driving (sedation). Avoid abrupt withdrawal (anxiety, insomnia, seizures, N&V). Combination with TCA or SNRI for refractory neuropathic pain (synergistic).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
Classα2δ Ca channel ligands
Gabapentin100-300mg TDS → 3600mg/day
Pregabalin75mg BD → 600mg/day
PregabalinSchedule 3 (controlled)
GabapentinSchedule 5 (controlled)
RenalGabapentin excreted renally; dose reduction
Side effectsSedation, dizziness, oedema, ataxia, weight gain
First-lineNICE for neuropathic pain

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
TCA (amitriptyline)Better for burning, less expensive
SNRI (duloxetine)Better for diabetic neuropathy, also antidepressant
TramadolBoth μ and SNRI; some risk of dependence

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Mechanism of gabapentin/pregabalin? A: α2δ ligand of voltage-gated Ca channels; reduces neurotransmitter release.
  1. 2. Q: First-line for what? A: Neuropathic pain (PHN, diabetic neuropathy, central pain, trigeminal neuralgia).
  1. 3. Q: Difference between them? A: Pregabalin has higher bioavailability (>90% vs 60%), faster titration, abuse potential.
  1. 4. Q: Maximum dose gabapentin? A: 3600mg/day (renally adjusted).
  1. 5. Q: Side effects? A: Sedation, dizziness, ataxia, oedema, weight gain, cognitive impairment.

▸ 9.6.06 Ketamine Nmda Antagonist Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Adjuvant Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Ketamine is an NMDA receptor antagonist used for refractory neuropathic cancer pain, opioid-sparing, and palliative sedation. Mechanism: NMDA receptor antagonism (central sensitisation), opioid receptor interaction, sodium channel blockade. Routes: oral, SC, IV, intranasal, topical. Dose: oral 10-25mg TDS (titrate); SC infusion 0.1-0.5 mg/kg/hr; IV 0.1-0.5 mg/kg/hr. Side effects: dissociation, hallucination, sedation, hypertension, salivation, urinary symptoms; monitor LFT. At subanaesthetic doses for analgesia. Limited evidence; specialist use.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Ketamine is a non-competitive NMDA receptor antagonist, used for refractory neuropathic pain (CRPS, post-herpetic, central, cancer pain with neuropathic component) and opioid-resistant pain. Low-dose ketamine 0.1-0.3mg/kg IV/SC, or 100-500mg/24h CSCI. Oral, SC, IV, intranasal. Side effects: dissociation, hallucinations, dysphoria (more with higher doses), sedation, hypertension, increased secretions, nausea, dizziness. Use with caution in: cardiovascular disease (HTN, ischaemia), schizophrenia, severe hepatic disease.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

ECG, BP, psychiatric history (psychosis), substance use, hepatic function. Monitor during administration: BP, HR, consciousness, emergence phenomena.

2. Management

Start low (e.g., 10-20mg SC/PO TDS), titrate. Add benzodiazepine (midazolam) to reduce dysphoria. Specialist use, often in inpatient/palliative care. Continue if benefit; discontinue if no response. Use with caution in substance use (abuse potential).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ClassNMDA receptor antagonist
UseRefractory neuropathic cancer pain, opioid-sparing
DoseOral 10-25mg TDS; SC/IV 0.1-0.5 mg/kg/hr
Side effectsDissociation, hallucination, sedation
TopicalAvailable; less systemic
SpecialistPalliative care / pain specialist

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Other NMDA antagonists (dextromethorphan, magnesium)Less evidence
OpioidsDifferent mechanism

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Ketamine mechanism? A: Non-competitive NMDA receptor antagonist; reduces central sensitisation.
  1. 2. Q: Indication? A: Refractory neuropathic pain, CRPS, opioid-resistant pain.
  1. 3. Q: Main side effects? A: Dissociation, hallucinations, dysphoria, sedation, hypertension, secretions.
  1. 4. Q: How to reduce dysphoria? A: Add benzodiazepine (midazolam, lorazepam).
  1. 5. Q: Routes? A: PO, SC, IV, intranasal, CSCI.

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.6.07 Lidocaine Iv Infusion Full

Related: [[../../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

[!info] Davidson alignment: Chapter 8: Pain and Palliative Care (24th Edition, Colvin, Fallon) — Adjuvant Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Lidocaine IV infusion is a parenteral adjuvant analgesic for refractory neuropathic pain (PHN, diabetic neuropathy, post-surgical, central pain, cancer pain). Mechanism: sodium channel blockade, anti-inflammatory, NMDA effects. Dose: 1-5mg/kg IV bolus then 1-3mg/kg/h infusion over 1-7 days (often 4-6h for trial). Contraindications: severe hepatic/renal failure, heart block, severe heart failure, epilepsy, pregnancy. Monitoring: ECG (QT, PR, QRS), BP, heart rate, neurological status (perioral numbness, tinnitus, seizures = toxicity). Side effects: CNS (perioral numbness, tinnitus, dizziness, tremor, seizures), cardiac (arrhythmias, heart block, hypotension), methaemoglobinaemia. Often used as inpatient trial to predict response; outpatient continuous infusion in select patients.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Lidocaine IV infusion is a parenteral adjuvant analgesic for refractory neuropathic pain (PHN, diabetic neuropathy, post-surgical, central pain, cancer pain). Mechanism: sodium channel blockade (NaV 1.7, 1.8, 1.9 in pain pathways), anti-inflammatory, NMDA effects. Dose: 1-5mg/kg IV bolus then 1-3mg/kg/h infusion over 1-7 days (often 4-6h for trial). Contraindications: severe hepatic/renal failure, heart block, severe heart failure, epilepsy, pregnancy. Side effects: CNS (perioral numbness, tinnitus, dizziness, tremor, seizures), cardiac (arrhythmias, heart block, hypotension, bradycardia), methaemoglobinaemia (rare). Monitoring: ECG (QT, PR, QRS), BP, heart rate, neurological status. Often used as inpatient trial to predict response.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

ECG (QT, PR, QRS) before and during infusion. Renal, hepatic function. Pain assessment, baseline, during, after. Drug levels (rarely used clinically). Methaemoglobin if cyanosis. Adverse effect monitoring: perioral numbness, tinnitus (early), tremor, seizures (toxicity).

2. Management

Indication: refractory neuropathic pain. Trial: often inpatient 4-6h; 1-3mg/kg IV infusion; assess pain relief. Continued: if response, consider CSCI lidocaine or repeat courses. Monitoring: ECG, BP, neurological status. Contraindications: severe hepatic/renal failure, heart block, heart failure, epilepsy. Side effects: perioral numbness, tinnitus (stop or reduce), dizziness, tremor, seizures (stop, supportive), cardiac (arrhythmias, hypotension). Combined with systemic opioid and adjuvant. Outpatient continuous infusion in select patients (rare, specialist).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
IndicationRefractory neuropathic pain (PHN, diabetic, post-surgical, central, cancer)
MechanismSodium channel blockade, anti-inflammatory, NMDA effects
Dose1-5mg/kg IV bolus, then 1-3mg/kg/h over 1-7 days (often 4-6h trial)
ContraindicationsSevere hepatic/renal failure, heart block, heart failure, epilepsy
ToxicityCNS (perioral numbness, tinnitus, dizziness, seizures), cardiac (arrhythmias, hypotension)
MonitoringECG, BP, heart rate, neurological status
TrialOften inpatient 4-6h to predict response
OutpatientContinuous infusion in select patients (rare, specialist)
EfficacyMixed evidence; some benefit in PHN, central pain, post-surgical

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Lidocaine patchLocalised, less systemic
Ketamine infusionAlternative NMDA antagonist
MexiletineOral analog, may have fewer side effects
IV lidocaineSame drug, different indication

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Mechanism? A: Sodium channel blockade (NaV 1.7, 1.8, 1.9); anti-inflammatory, NMDA.
  1. 2. Q: Dose? A: 1-3mg/kg/h IV over 4-6h trial (or 1-7 days).
  1. 3. Q: Contraindications? A: Severe hepatic/renal failure, heart block, heart failure, epilepsy.
  1. 4. Q: Early toxicity? A: Perioral numbness, tinnitus, dizziness, tremor.
  1. 5. Q: Monitoring? A: ECG, BP, neurological status; cardiac monitoring for high dose.

▸ 9.6.08 Snris As Analgesics Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Adjuvant Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Serotonin-noradrenaline reuptake inhibitors (SNRIs) are adjuvant analgesics for neuropathic pain (first-line per NICE). Examples: duloxetine (60mg/day), venlafaxine (75-225mg/day). Duloxetine is first-line specifically for painful diabetic neuropathy. Mechanism: serotonin and noradrenaline reuptake inhibition, enhancing descending inhibitory pathways. Side effects: nausea (most common, take with food), dry mouth, dizziness, sexual dysfunction, sweating, hyponatraemia (SIADH in elderly), hypertension (venlafaxine high doses), serotonin syndrome with MAOIs, withdrawal on stopping. Avoid in: uncontrolled hypertension, MAOI, severe liver disease, severe renal disease.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Serotonin-noradrenaline reuptake inhibitors (SNRIs — duloxetine, venlafaxine, milnacipran) are first-line for neuropathic pain (NICE), especially duloxetine for painful diabetic neuropathy (FDA/EMA-approved). Duloxetine 60-120mg/day; venlafaxine 75-225mg/day (MR). Effective for diabetic neuropathy, fibromyalgia, chronic musculoskeletal pain. Side effects: nausea, dry mouth, constipation, sweating, sexual dysfunction, hypertension (venlafaxine, especially high dose), hepatotoxicity (duloxetine — avoid in liver disease), withdrawal syndrome (gradual taper).

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

LFT (duloxetine), BP (venlafaxine), renal function. Monitor for serotonin syndrome (with tramadol, TCAs, MAOIs, triptans).

2. Management

Start duloxetine 30mg/day, titrate to 60-120mg/day. Take with food. Avoid in: liver disease, severe renal failure, uncontrolled hypertension, MAOI use. Counsel on withdrawal (gradual taper, never abrupt).

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ClassSNRI
ExamplesDuloxetine 60mg, venlafaxine 75-225mg
Mechanism5-HT + NA reuptake inhibition
DuloxetineFirst-line for PDN
Side effectsNausea, dizziness, dry mouth, sexual dysfunction
CIUncontrolled hypertension, MAOI, severe liver/renal
SerotoninAvoid with MAOI, linezolid

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
TCAsMore analgesic efficacy, more side effects
GabapentinDifferent mechanism, often combined

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: First-line SNRI for neuropathic pain? A: Duloxetine (FDA/EMA-approved for diabetic neuropathy).
  1. 2. Q: Why duloxetine in diabetic neuropathy? A: SNRI; treats both pain and depression (common comorbidity).
  1. 3. Q: Risk vs TCA? A: Less sedation, no anticholinergic, no cardiac conduction effects.
  1. 4. Q: Side effects? A: Nausea, dry mouth, sexual dysfunction, hypertension, hepatotoxicity (duloxetine).
  1. 5. Q: Serotonin syndrome risk? A: Yes, with tramadol, SSRIs, TCAs, MAOIs, triptans.

  • See clinical features

▸ 9.6.09 Tcas As Analgesics Full

Related: [[../../Pain and Palliative Care MOC]], [[../../Davidson Chapter 8 - Pain and Palliative Care Hierarchy]]

Davidson 24e Ch 8 (Colvin, Fallon) — Adjuvant Analgesics

1. 1. Definition

Tricyclic antidepressants (TCAs) are adjuvant analgesics used for neuropathic pain (first-line per NICE). Mechanism: serotonin and noradrenaline reuptake inhibition (SNRI-like), sodium channel blockade, NMDA receptor antagonism. Examples: amitriptyline (most used, sedating), nortriptyline (less sedating), imipramine, desipramine. Dose: 10-25mg nocte (amitriptyline), titrate up to 75-150mg. Onset 1-2 weeks. Side effects: anticholinergic (dry mouth, urinary retention, constipation, blurred vision), sedation, cardiac (QTc, conduction — overdose toxic), orthostatic hypotension. Contraindicated in: recent MI, arrhythmias, narrow-angle glaucoma, urinary retention.

2. 2. Clinical Features and Presentation

Tricyclic antidepressants (TCAs — amitriptyline, nortriptyline, imipramine, desipramine) have analgesic efficacy in neuropathic pain at lower doses than antidepressant. Mechanism: serotonin/NA reuptake inhibition, sodium channel blockade, NMDA antagonism, opioid receptor modulation. First-line for neuropathic pain (NICE). Dose: amitriptyline 10-25mg nocte, titrate to 25-150mg nocte. Side effects: anticholinergic (dry mouth, constipation, urinary retention, blurred vision, tachycardia), sedation, orthostatic hypotension, weight gain, cardiac (QRS widening, arrhythmias — caution in elderly, IHD, conduction disease), seizure threshold lowering (high doses), suicide risk in overdose.

3. 3. Investigation and Management Summary

1. Investigations

ECG before (QTc, conduction disease), especially in elderly or cardiac history. Renal, liver function. Lying/standing BP. Monitor for anticholinergic side effects.

2. Management

Start low (10-25mg amitriptyline in elderly, 25-50mg in adults), titrate by 10-25mg weekly to 25-150mg nocte. Take at bedtime. Avoid in: elderly with falls, cardiac disease (recent MI, arrhythmia), narrow-angle glaucoma, urinary retention, mania, severe liver disease, MAOI use (washout 14 days). Counsel on overdose risk.

4. 4. FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

CategoryKey Points
ClassTCA; SNRI + Na channel + NMDA
ExamplesAmitriptyline, nortriptyline
DoseAmitriptyline 10-25mg nocte, titrate to 75-150mg
Onset1-2 weeks
Side effectsAnticholinergic, sedation, cardiac, orthostatic
CIRecent MI, arrhythmia, narrow-angle glaucoma, urinary retention
UseFirst-line for neuropathic (NICE)
MaudsleyStart low, titrate, therapeutic trial ≥6 weeks

5. 5. Differential Diagnosis

DifferentialDistinguishing Feature
Gabapentin/pregabalinBetter tolerated, especially elderly
SNRILess sedation, no anticholinergic
SSRINot analgesic

6. 6. Viva Questions

  1. 1. Q: Why amitriptyline for neuropathic pain? A: TCA, multiple mechanisms (5HT/NA reuptake, Na channel, NMDA), effective at low doses.
  1. 2. Q: Why elderly avoid? A: Anticholinergic side effects, falls, orthostatic hypotension, cardiac conduction.
  1. 3. Q: First-line with gabapentin? A: Yes, both NICE first-line; combination if inadequate.
  1. 4. Q: Best time to take? A: Bedtime (sedating, helps sleep).
  1. 5. Q: Contraindication? A: Recent MI, conduction disease, narrow-angle glaucoma, urinary retention, MAOI use.

7. 7. Confusions and Mnemonics

  • See clinical features
